Preface
This thesis is submitted as a partial fulﬁlment of the requirements for graduation of the degree
of Master of Science in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The project is
credited as 60 ECTS points, equally split between the two students. The work has been carried
out in the autumn semester of 2021, from the 31st of August to the 6th of February.
This project has been conducted in a continuation of the work and ﬁndings regarding brittle frac-
ture in accordance to EN 1993-1-10 performed earlier in 2021 by T. Pedersen and A. Stergiou, in
hopes of further development of fracture mechanical approaches for oﬀshore steel structures for
the wind energy industry.
Thomas J. Pedersen
Alexander S. W. Stergiou

==PAGE:12==
==PAGE:13==
Abstract
Steel substructures for oﬀshore wind turbines are subject to severe dynamic loading, inducing cyclic
stresses within the structure. The cyclic stresses result in damage accumulation in the form of crack
nucleation and propagation which ultimately leads to structural collapse, either by brittle fracture,
ductile rupture or a combination of the two. The current design procedure for investigation of
damage accumulation is based on the S-N curve approach. The subsequent brittle fracture assess-
ment is performed independently of the actual fatigue damage accumulation, despite fatigue crack
growth generally preceding fracture. Therefore, the conventional procedures will be challenged by
an Engineering Critical Assessment (ECA) which will incorporate both phenomena in one analysis.
This thesis considers a circumferential butt weld in a monopile structure with an external
semi-elliptical surface ﬂaw representing an undercut, which is conservatively assumed planar. A
detailed linear elastic fracture mechanical crack growth model is established, including a represen-
tative stress intensity solution, weld geometry correction factors, through-thickness residual stress
distributions and case-speciﬁc crack growth parameters from recognised standards and industry
reports to ensure accuracy and precision. A numerical tool has been developed, dubbed ECAT,
which performs a crack growth analysis based on detailed fatigue load data, while concurrently
assessing the risk of failure. Failure evaluations are performed with Failure Assessment Diagrams,
which enable the application of linear elastic fracture mechanics to assess non-linear failure mech-
anisms, by including the interaction between brittle fracture and plastic collapse. Supplementary
detailed numerical models are established in order to assess the precision of the analytical fracture
mechanical parameters used in ECAT. This consists of ﬁnite element analyses in Abaqus, where
geometry functions are determined for representative cracks in a monopile, as well as a detailed
evaluation of the weld stress magniﬁcation factor.
The ECAs with an initial ﬂaw depth of a = 0.5 mm (a/t = 0.0065), estimated a fatigue life
ranging between 25-30 years, depending on the load application sequence and method. The ECA
approach assumes the ﬂaw to be a planar crack, neglecting the fatigue initiation period, which
generally constitutes 10-30% of the fatigue life. The ECAs showed signiﬁcant sensitivity to the
initial crack size. The conventional S-N approach estimated greater fatigue life, ranging between
28-35 years. The conventional brittle fracture check proved to severely underestimate fatigue crack
growth. Based on the ECAs, brittle fracture was the governing failure mechanism at the end of all
analyses, indicating risk of brittle fracture prior to reaching the end of the fatigue design life. The
conventional brittle fracture approach thus yielded unconservative results for all load application
methods. The FEA results indicated that optimisation of both the analytical expressions for the
stress intensity factors, as well as the stress magniﬁcation factors, could potentially be achieved by
applying FEA based fracture mechanical parameters to model crack growth.
Key words:
Monopiles, S-N Curves, Fatigue, Fracture Mechanics, Brittle Fracture, LEFM, ECA, FEA
==PAGE:14==
==PAGE:15==
Resumé
Stålkonstruktioner til havvindmøller er udsat for betydelig dynamisk belastning, hvilket induc-
erer cykliske spændinger i konstruktionen. De cykliske spændinger resulterer i udvikling af skader
i form af revnedannelse og -udvikling, som i sidste ende fører til strukturelt kollaps, enten ved
sprødbrud, duktilt brud eller en kombination af de to. Den nuværende designprocedure til un-
dersøgelse af skadesakkumulering er baseret på S-N-kurver. Den efterfølgende tjek af sprødbrud
foretages uafhængigt af den faktiske udmattelse, trods udmattelsesrevner normalt udvikles før
bruddet. Derfor vil de konventionelle procedurer blive udfordret af en Engineering Critical Assess-
ment (ECA), som vil inddrage udmattelse og sprødbrud i én analyse.
I denne afhandling behandles en rundsøm i en monopæl med en ekstern semi-elliptisk overﬂade-
fejl, der repræsenterer en sidekærv, som konservativt antages at være en plan fejl. Der opstilles
en detaljeret lineær elastisk brudmekanisk revnevækstmodel, herunder en repræsentativ spænd-
ingsintensitetsfaktor, korrektionsfaktorer for svejsegeometri, residualspændingsfordelinger gennem
tykkelsen og case-speciﬁkke revnevækstparametre fra anerkendte standarder og industrirapporter
for at sikre nøjagtighed og præcision. Der er udviklet et numerisk værktøj, ECAT, som udfører
en analyse af revnevækst på grundlag af detaljerede udmattelsesbelastnings data, samtidig med at
risikoen for sprødbrud vurderes. Risikoen udføres med Failure Assessment Diagrams, som gør det
muligt at anvende lineær elastisk brudmekanik til at vurdere ikke-lineære brudmekanismer ved at
inddrage interaktionen mellem sprødt brud og plastisk kollaps. Der opstilles supplerende detal-
jerede numeriske modeller for at vurdere præcisionen af de analytiske brudmekaniske parametre,
der anvendes i ECAT. Dette består af ﬁnitte element analyser i Abaqus, hvor geometrifunktioner
bestemmes for repræsentative revner i en monopæl, samt en detaljeret evaluering af spændingsin-
tensitetsforstørrelsesfaktoren.
ECA’er med en initial fejldybde på a = 0.5 mm (a/t = 0, 0065) anslog en udmattelseslevetid på
25-30 år, afhængigt af lastsekvensen og metoden. I ECA-metoden antages det, at fejlen er en plan
revne, idet der ses bort fra perioden for udmattelsesinitiering, som generelt udgør 10-30% af udmat-
telseslevetiden. ECA’erne viste en betydelig følsomhed over for den initielle revnes størrelse. Den
konventionelle S-N-metode anslog en længere udmattelseslevetid på 28-35 år. Det konventionelle
sprødbrudstjek viste sig at undervurdere revneudviklingen. Baseret på ECA’erne var sprødbrud
den dominerende brudmekanisme ved alle analyser, hvilket indikerer risiko for sprødbrud inden
for udmattelseslevetiden. Det konventionelle tjek for sprødbrud resulterede således i ukonservative
resultater for alle belastningsmetoder. FEA-resultaterne viste, at der potentielt kunne opnås en
optimering af både de analytiske udtryk for spændingsintensitetsfaktorerne og spændingsinten-
sitetsforstørrelsesfaktoren ved at anvende FEA-baserede brudmekaniske parametre til modellering
af revnevækst.

1
Introduction
1.1
Introduction
The ﬁrst oﬀshore wind farms were installed 30 years ago, with the number of installed turbines
increasing each year ever since. At the start of the millennium, oﬀshore wind energy made up
approximately 1% of the total global wind energy and 20 years later that number has jumped to
almost 5% [1]. With the current ambitions for the green transition and large sums of money being
invested by governments worldwide, experts are predicting a continuous growth of the oﬀshore
wind sector which in its current state is only 2% of the necessary size to reach the mid-century
climate goals [2].
Figure 1.1: Installed oﬀshore wind capacity between 1998 and 2020 [1][3]
It is estimated that within the next 5 years, 38 GW of European wind farms will have reached
the end of their design life, the vast majority belonging to onshore wind [4]. Nevertheless, the
current trends for oﬀshore wind indicate that before any climate goals are reached, a large num-
ber of oﬀshore turbines will reach the end of their design life. The current plans for a majority
of turbines reaching their designated design life, is decommissioning regardless of their condition,
despite several early projects showing life-extension is possible [5]. Repowering is also a possibility
for oﬀshore wind, which for onshore wind costs 20-30% of a new turbine. Repowering will be more
challenging for the oﬀshore sector, but a techno-economic study performed by Mehmanparast et
al. concludes that it is more than likely to be a ﬁnancially viable option and will only become
1
==PAGE:34==
1. Introduction
easier and more cost eﬀective as experience is gained [6].
Lifetime extension of wind turbines also requires suﬃcient structural integrity of the founda-
tions. Inspections and fracture mechanics have been employed for several years in the oﬀshore oil
and gas sector with great success. Similar methods could also be utilised for for the oﬀshore wind
sector, allowing for inspections of foundations nearing the end of design life.
The increasing accuracy and probability of detection for newer and more advanced non-destructive
testing methods, such as Phased Array Ultrasonic Testing (PAUT), are increasing the reliability
of using fracture mechanics. Advanced methods allow for more accurate sizing of ﬂaws, which
combined with fracture mechanics, can determine the acceptability of fabrication ﬂaws regarding
the fatigue life and also determine the remaining fatigue life of a ﬂawed structure during service [7].
1.1.1
Oﬀshore Wind Farm Foundations
The greatest challenge for oﬀshore wind farms as opposed to onshore, is the marine environment.
The sea not only induces a new set of loads, both wave and current, and constitutes a corrosive
environment but also increases the moment the foundations must withstand, due to the distance
from the rotor nacelle assembly (RNA) to the seabed. All these factors contribute to oﬀshore
structures being more demanding and challenging structures than the typical onshore structures.
Several diﬀerent substructure types are used as foundations for oﬀshore wind turbines, three of
which can be seen in Figure 1.2.
Figure 1.2: Wind turbine foundation types, left to right: Gravity base, Monopile, Jacket Figure:
© COWI A/S
The monopile (MP), is currently the dominant support structure in the oﬀshore wind indus-
try. The simple design has allowed the industry to systematise and standardise the production,
2
==PAGE:35==
1. Introduction
transportation and installation procedures. Figure 1.3 gives an overview of the foundation type
distribution for oﬀshore wind farms as of 2020 [8]. In the ﬁgure it is seen that over 80% of all
oﬀshore wind turbines were supported by monopiles. This superior track record makes the MP
very attractive for contractors looking to develop new oﬀshore wind farms.
Figure 1.3: Distribution of foundation types for oﬀshore wind (2020) [8]
With the increased demand for oﬀshore wind power, the technological advances have resulted
in turbine sizes increasing rapidly. The current generation of turbines have capacities of up to
15MW. The increased capacity requires larger rotor diameters as seen on Siemens Gamesa’s SG
14-222 turbine with a diameter of 222m [9] and Vestas’ V236-15.0 MW turbine with a diameter
of 236m [10]. XXL monopiles are currently being designed and fabricated to support the newest
generation of turbines. The MPs need to accommodate the increased turbine tower sizes as well as
the larger loads. Additionally, as an increasing number oﬀshore wind farm projects being planned
the availability of suitable locations with shallow water depths is shrinking, especially in estab-
lished oﬀshore wind areas such as Europe. Therefore support structures need to accommodate
both larger turbines and increased water depths.
Presently, some of the largest MPs belong to the German oﬀshore wind farm (OWF) project
Arcadis Ost 1, which are currently in production, are to be installed at water depths up to 46m
[11]. Studies are being performed to investigate the feasibility of using MPs at water depths up to
65m [12]. Figure 1.4 shows the industry trends for the MP foundations from 2008 up to 2020 [13]
with expected MP sizes from Bladt for ’22 [14].
1.1.1.1
The Monopile Foundation
The monopile foundation is generally deﬁned as a long and slender steel pipe. This allows for
a relatively standardised fabrication, due to the simplicity of the geometry, as opposed to jacket
structure where no exact industry layout is established. While jacket structures are assembled by
a large number of challenging welds, the monopile can be manufactured with a reduced amount of
semi-automated welds. Figure 1.5 shows a simpliﬁed illustration of the monopile manufacturing
3
==PAGE:36==
1. Introduction
Figure 1.4: Industry trends for MP diameters and lengths
process.
Figure 1.5: Monopile can assembly process
As seen in Figure 1.5, the starting point of each can section is a ﬂat steel plate. The plate is
cold rolled to form a cylinder and the two ends are joined by a longitudinal weld. The can section
is then joined with another can by a circumferential weld. All subsequent cans are likewise added
until the full length is achieved. An example of a monopile can be seen in Figure 1.6.
Figure 1.6: Yunlin OWF MP (Weight: 1.732 t, Diameter: 8 m, Length: 93 m) [15]
The sketch in Figure 1.7 illustrates 4 cans forming part of an MP. The longitudinal welds are
4
==PAGE:37==
1. Introduction
staggered by at least 30◦to avoid interaction and to ensure that the monopile is not weak in a
speciﬁc orientation [16].
Figure 1.7: Sketch of 4 MP cans welded together
The majority of the welds performed on monopiles are double-sided full penetration butt welds
(FPBW). Therefore, these types of welds will be the main focus of this thesis. An example of
an FPBW in a 90mm plate is presented by Mehmanparast et.al [17] which is a typical weld size
for an MP, and can be seen in Figure 1.8. The plate is welded from both sides, by multiple weld
passes. Monopiles are typically fabricated by submerged arc welding (SAW), which is an easily
automatised and relatively quick welding procedure.
Figure 1.8: 90mm weldment typical of an oﬀshore wind monopile structure [17]
In locations where fatigue damage is critical for the overall structural capacity, such as welded
connections between cans and ﬂanges, dressing or grinding of the weld can be performed. The weld
notches at the surface are smoothed/removed, which improves the strength and fatigue resistance
of the joint.
1.1.1.2
Loads on Oﬀshore Structures
Loads on oﬀshore structures can be divided into two main categories: gravity loads and environ-
mental loads with corresponding derivatives of the two. The general loads of oﬀshore structures
5
==PAGE:38==
1. Introduction
within the two categories are listed in the following [18]:
Gravity loads
Environmental loads
Structural dead loads
Wind loads
Facility dead loads
Wave loads
Live loads
Current loads
Transport and Installation Loads
Buoyancy loads
Seismic loads
The environmental loads are often governing regarding the design of oﬀshore structures, there-
fore, an accurate prediction is important. This thesis will primarily focus on the combined envi-
ronmental loads, dead loads and transport and installation (T&I) loads.
1.1.1.3
Structural Design Veriﬁcation
To assure the integrity of a structure, the designer must verify the capacity against all possible
failure scenarios. A set of integrity checks must be performed, which generally consist of: Ultimate
Limit State (ULS), Accidental Limit State (ALS) and Fatigue Limit State (FLS). The ULS and
ALS cases consist structural stress and stability veriﬁcation and the FLS is a structural lifetime
damage veriﬁcation.
1.1.1.3.1
Ultimate Limit State
The ULS design checks represents the extreme load scenario. For oﬀshore structures this is repre-
sented by the worst storm, i.e. the largest wave and wind combination to be expected during the
lifetime of the structure. Usually the check is passed if it is proven that plasticity is avoided in
critical locations and that global nor local instability, i.e. buckling will occur.
1.1.1.3.2
Accidental Limit State
The ALS design checks can cover earthquakes, vessel collisions, explosions and impacts from aerial
vehicles, during normal operating conditions. The checks are passed if total collapse of the structure
is avoided, i.e. local buckling and yielding is permitted as long as the global integrity is assured.
1.1.1.3.3
Fatigue Limit State
The FLS design check represents the damage accumulation throughout the lifetime of the structure.
Under dynamic loading, which oﬀshore structures encounter, fatigue damage accumulates and the
FLS check veriﬁes that no point of the structure will fail due to accumulated fatigue damage during
the design life of the structure.
1.2
Objective
The objective of this thesis is to investigate the possibilities of employing fracture mechanics as
a method of determining and optimising the design life of an oﬀshore structure in the wind en-
ergy sector. This encompasses both fatigue damage accumulation and brittle fracture, phenomena
6
==PAGE:39==
1. Introduction
that are directly related to crack propagation and therefore can be described by fracture mechanics.
The current method of determining the fatigue life of oﬀshore monopiles is based on the use of
S-N curves, which builds on a simpliﬁed damage accumulation assumption, and may in some cases
be overly conservative. Given the inaccessibility of monopiles, they are in general designed for a
design life without inspection, and cracks will not be detected prior to the decommissioning phase.
Therefore, establishing a reliable method of applying fracture mechanics to a realistic structural
detail, in time can be applied during the fabrication phase, with more accurate and reliable NDT
methods being developed. This could potentially allow for tailored design life assessments for indi-
vidual monopiles. The method can also be used for monopile structures nearing decommissioning,
extending the reliable service life increasing the ﬁnancial and environmental gains and thereby
optimising the structures.
The current procedures for assessment of brittle fracture are very simple and elementary, to
accommodate for use on all steel structures. To address this, the brittle fracture criterion is in-
corporated in the fracture mechanical model applied in this thesis, in order to introduce a greater
level of detail and accuracy in the brittle fracture assessment.
Additionally, brittle fracture and fatigue are coherent, with fatigue damage accumulation usu-
ally preceding brittle fracture. Currently, the fatigue design and the brittle fracture veriﬁcation
consists of two separate, independent integrity checks. In this thesis, the two structural phenomena
will be assessed in connection with each other, resembling the true interaction between the two.
1.3
Scope of Work
This thesis will cover aspects of fatigue speciﬁcally related to monopile oﬀshore structures. COWI
A/S has supplied an input report consisting of a detailed monopile design, including design loads.
To be able to optimise an existing process, a thorough understanding of that process is necessary.
Therefore the ﬁrst sections of this thesis will cover the current methods and practices of fatigue
and brittle fracture design in oﬀshore monopile structures, in depth, to allow for identiﬁcation of
sub-optimal procedures and subsequent possibilities of optimisation.
Following this, the ﬁeld of fracture mechanics will be introduced and related to the supplied input.
An investigation of relevant fracture mechanical theory and norms will form the foundation of an
extensive deterministic linear elastic crack growth analysis, combined with an elastic-plastic frac-
ture mechanical failure assessment. The maximum expected initial ﬂaw sizes will be determined
based on the governing norm requirements and form the basis of the analysis. The analysis will be
performed using a set of developed Matlab scripts employing an algorithm resembling an explicit
integration scheme. This developed program will be benchmarked against recognised commercial
fracture mechanics software, CrackWISE ®, for a generic simple case of a semi-elliptical surface
crack.
7
==PAGE:40==
1. Introduction
In addition, numerical commercial software, Abaqus, will be utilised to validate the analytical
approach and explore the opportunity for optimisation, providing more accurate case-speciﬁc pa-
rameters. Finally the fracture mechanics approach will be compared to and challenge both the
S-N curve fatigue design method as well as the current procedures for design for brittle fracture,
subsequently investigating possibilities for life-time extension.
1.4
Preliminary Studies
Prior to this thesis, a fracture mechanical investigation was conducted of the brittle fracture as-
sessment procedures presented in EN 1993-1-10, which is broadly applied in the steel industry.
The study is titled: Fracture Mechanical Background Investigation of Steel Element Thickness in
EN 1993-1-10[19]. The ﬁndings of the preliminary study will be referenced through out this thesis
regarding current brittle fracture design procedures.
1.5
Thesis Structure
Figure 1.9 shows the structure of the thesis. Block 1 deals with the conventional methodology of
designing monopiles for the oﬀshore wind industry, covering theory, literature, norms and demon-
strating a structural assessment. Block 2 covers a fracture mechanical approach to the design
of monopiles for the oﬀshore wind industry, introducing relevant theory, literature, norms and
structural assessment performing and ECA followed by supplementary FEA. The ﬁnal section
encompasses the evaluation, discussion and conclusion of the two methodologies.
Figure 1.9: Thesis structure
8
==PAGE:41==
2
Fundamental Theory of
Conventional Procedures
2.1
Welded Oﬀshore Substructures
While welding makes for an easy and elegant joint between two components, it also results in
speciﬁc items to be considered. The welding procedure introduces weld notch stress concentration
factors, risk of weld ﬂaws, residual stresses and a change in the micro structure of the material. All
of which inﬂuence the behaviour and mechanical properties of the joint, and need to be considered
during design and fabrication, especially with regards to fatigue.
2.1.1
Residual Stresses
Residual stresses are built-in stresses within a structural component. Residual stresses are self-
equilibrating, and are present in the component even in an un-loaded state, i.e. permanently acting
within the material. Residual stresses inﬂuence the fatigue behaviour and fracture strength of a ma-
terial, therefore are a major factor to be considered when assessing both the design life and failure
of a structure. Residual stresses are usually categorised as secondary stresses that can be deﬁned
as displacement-controlled, which diﬀerentiate from primary stresses which are load-controlled [20].
Typically for MPs, residual stresses are introduced during the manufacturing process, during
cold rolling and welding. The extreme temperatures imposed by the welding process induce residual
stresses in the material. The residual stresses occur due to the rapid temperature increase during
the welding procedure, and the subsequent cooling of the material after the weld-pass.
Due to the large temperature diﬀerences between the hot weld material and the cold parent
material, thermal expansion is restricted, and compressive stresses form. Inversely when the hot
weld metal cools down and retracts, the surrounding material prohibits the volumetric change, and
tensile stresses are introduced. Figure 2.1 depicts the formation of residual stresses alongside the
temperature distribution during the welding sequence.
A way of depicting the formation of residual stresses is the three bar conﬁguration, presented by
Michaleris [21]. Three steel bars are placed between two supports, as seen in Figure 2.2. Heating
of the middle bar (2) results in thermal expansion, causing the expanded bar to attempt to push
the support up. The outer bars (1 & 3) experience tension, trying to restrain the support from
moving, causing a compressive stress in bar 2. When cooling the bar back to ambient temperature,
the bar will attempt to shrink to a lesser height, due to plastic deformations induced under the
compressive stresses. As for a welded joint, bar 2 represents the weld material and the two other
9
==PAGE:42==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
Figure 2.1: Residual stress development sequence
bars represent the parent material. After a welding procedure, the weld and parent material is
fused, and thus there is a coupling between the two. Therefore, the weld material is prohibited
from contracting to the reduced state, and tensile stresses are introduced in the weld material.
The surrounding base material reacts to the tensile stress, and compressive stresses form.
Figure 2.2: Three bar conﬁguration visualising the formation of residual stresses
The residual stresses in and around a weld are somewhat unknown although it is commonly
accepted that residual stresses induced by welding can reach a magnitude of the yield strength
of the material [22]. An example of the transverse and longitudinal residual stress distributions
of a FPBW are presented in Figure 2.3. Longitudinal residual stresses are generally greater than
transverse residual stresses although the transverse stresses may increase in magnitude, in cases
where external constraints prohibits weld shrinkage [23]. Note that the distributions presented in
Figure 2.3 represent the residual stresses near the surface. The residual stresses also vary through
the thickness of the member, which will be further elaborated in Section 6.3.7.
The magnitude of the residual stresses has a signiﬁcant impact on the fatigue resistance of a
component. Large residual stresses mean the structural component is in a constant tensile stress
state, increasing the rate at which cracks can propagate through the material. The constant tensile
stress state therefore also means that even compressive stress ranges eﬀectively are in tension.
10
==PAGE:43==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 2.3: Longitudinal and transverse residual stress distributions in a FPBW
2.1.2
Change in Material Micro Structure
The welding procedure reaches temperatures exceeding the melting point of the parent material.
Exposing the parent material to these rapid and extreme temperature changes leads to a change in
the micro structure of the material. The term heat aﬀected zone (HAZ) describes the weld region
in the immediate vicinity of the weld joint, as seen in Figure 2.4. The size of the HAZ depends on
the magnitude of the heat input, the duration of the temperature exposure and the properties of
the material itself. A higher temperature and a longer exposure time lead to a larger HAZ [24].
Figure 2.4: A schematic diagram of the sub-zones of the heat aﬀected zone
Generally, the HAZ can be split up into a number of sub-zones, depending on how close they
are to the weld. The region of the weld material is the liquid region, and it is adjoined by the
fusion boundary, also denoted the solid-liquid boundary. This is the boundary layer at which the
liquid metal fuses with the solid parent material. Adjacent is the grain growth zone. This part
of the parent material has experienced the highest temperatures which results in a coarser grain
structure. This micro-structure exhibits an increased hardness and a lower fracture toughness,
11
==PAGE:44==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
i.e. a more brittle behaviour. While the recrystallised and tempered zones have experienced high
temperatures, they are less aﬀected, as their respective distance from the weld is greater.
2.1.3
Welding Flaws
Various ﬂaws and errors can be introduced when welding. The presence of ﬂaws can introduce
singularities, voids and dis-joined planes from which cracks can nucleate and propagate. Many
welds performed on MPs are semi-automated, which reduces but does not eliminate the risk of
human error leading to ﬂaws.
Figure 2.5: Typical ﬂaws found in and near welds
There are numerous types of defects that can be introduced into a component by welding, such
as undercutting, inclusions and lack of fusion. Examples of these can be seen in Figure 2.5. There
are very strict rules with very little tolerance of weld defects when designing welded structures,
and even more so for oﬀshore structures. Common for all these defects are that they signiﬁcantly
increase the risk of crack development, and reduce the fatigue life of the structure. Flaws are
usually split into two categories, planar and 3D ﬂaws. 3D ﬂaws, such as inclusions and porosity
are not as critical as planar ﬂaws which includes cracks, lack of fusion, severe undercuts or lack
of penetration. This has been demonstrated by Maddox [25] where transverse butt welds with
signiﬁcant internal defects were present, but failure initiated at the toe of the weld, as shown in
Figure 2.6.
(a) Slag inclusion
(b) Porosity
(c) Lack of penetration
Figure 2.6: Butt weld failures with defects [25]
Cases (a) and (b) failed from a crack originating from the weld toe (planar defect) despite the
presence of slag and porosity (3-D defects). Case (c) has suﬀered from failure initiating from the
12
==PAGE:45==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
weld root, due to lack of penetration which resulted in a planar defect at the root, despite severe
undercutting at the bottom left and large transition slopes from the base material to the ﬁller
material.
2.1.4
Weld Notch Stress Concentration
Stress concentrations are points at which the stress magnitude locally is increased relative to the
nominal stress. Stress concentrations occur where there is either a discontinuity or a change in
stiﬀness. This could be a hole in a plate, a thickness change or a weld notch. The latter can be
seen depicted in Figure 2.7, showing a ﬁllet weld joining two plates. The bottom plate is loaded by
a uniform tensile stress. For a clean plate with no weld, the nominal stress is constant throughout
the thickness, but for the welded plate, a discontinuity and added stiﬀness in the form of a weld
notch is present. The stress increases at the surface due to the discontinuities where a peak stress
is found, which rapidly diverges back to a magnitude close to the nominal stress as it advances
through the thickness.
Figure 2.7: Illustration of stress distribution at weld toe
While stress concentrations not only are present at welds, the stress concentrations introduced
by the welds are of signiﬁcant concern in combination with the other complications that occur
at welds. Therefore, strict requirements regarding weld geometry and quality are prescribed in
fabrication norms, which will be elaborated in Section 6.2.
2.2
The Fatigue Mechanism
When a component composed of a ductile metal is loaded "statically" by a gradually increasing
load, the ﬁnal rupture of the material is preceded by very large plastic strains. This type of failure
is advantageous seen from a structural designers perspective, since it displays an easily detectable
indication of impending failure in the form of large plastic deformations, while still maintaining
excess capacity before rupture. However, if the same component is subjected to repeated loads,
failure may occur as a result of stresses much lower than the elastic limit of the material. This is
known as structural fatigue.
13
==PAGE:46==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
Fatigue is deﬁned as the weakening of a material over time, caused by cyclic loading. The cause
of the weakening of the material is progressive structural damage in the form of cracks, which can
form and grow at almost all load magnitudes, depending on the material and environmental con-
ditions. Fatigue damage is therefore virtually inevitable in structures subject to dynamic loading.
Due to the lack of plastic deformation, fatigue damage is more challenging to detect. Herein lies the
particular danger of fatigue failure, since no signiﬁcant warning signs such as dimensional changes
or large deformations occur prior to failure.
2.2.1
Fatigue in Welded Monopile Structures
A section cut of a monopile is sketched in Figure 2.8. Generally speaking, there are two types of
welds in MPs; circumferential welds (2) and longitudinal welds (3). Since bending moments are the
prevailing loads on MPs, the MP will be in a bending stress state as depicted on the ﬁgure. Cracks
subject to loading perpendicular to the crack-plane are the most critical with respect fatigue crack
growth. In the ﬁgure, three possible transverse crack cases are presented. Case 1 is a crack formed
in the base material, i.e. with no welds in the vicinity. These cracks can occur due to irregularities
in the base material or indentations caused during fabrication. Case 2 is a crack parallel to the
circumferential weld, typically found in the HAZ. These cracks usually nucleate due to the stress
concentration at the weld toe combined with the transverse residual stresses. Case 3 is a crack
transverse to the longitudinal weld, which develops due surface irregularities near and on the weld
bead combined with the longitudinal residual stresses [26].
Figure 2.8: MP Can section cut, with the three prominent crack cases
Weld notch stress concentrations positioned in the HAZ combined with large transverse tensile
residual stresses makes case 2 the design governing fatigue detail for the MPs.
Based on this
reasoning, the transverse crack at the toe of the circumferential FPBW is chosen to be the repre-
sentative case for the investigations through out this thesis.
14
==PAGE:47==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
The progressive deterioration of the structure through crack propagation starting from shallow
cracks and irregularities does not develop in a linear fashion. Figure 2.9 presents the typical shape
of the crack growth curve over time. The curve is characterised by the very slow crack growth rate
in the beginning, which over time gradually accelerates until reaching an almost vertical tangent.
Initially the cracks are very small and as the crack grows during service, the load transferring
cross-sectional area is reduced, further increasing the local stress, accelerating the crack growth
further. This phenomenon is especially problematic with regards to inspection of a structure, since
the cracks are detected at a relatively late stage of the crack development, which means close to
the point of fracture [27].
Figure 2.9: Sketch of typical crack development curve
The fatigue life of a structure can be separated into three distinct phases, which are usually de-
noted as: fatigue Initiation, crack propagation and structural failure [26]. This thesis will primarily
focus on the 2 last phases, crack propagation and structural failure.
2.2.2
Fatigue Initiation
Fatigue initiation describes the formation of a microscopic crack, initiated by material separation
on a atomic scale, prior to the crack becoming a visible macrocrack. Standard crack propagation
formulae can not be utilised to describe the crack in the initiation phase.
The initial micro-
cracks nucleate from repeated localised plastic strain and occurs on crystallographic slip planes,
which cause intrusions on free surfaces. During this phase, the micro-cracks and intrusions will
propagate at a 45◦angle. The initiation phase, especially for welded joints, is shorter than the crack
propagation phase, taking up 10-30% of the entire fatigue life [28], and can therefore conservatively
be omitted from an analysis [26]. Fine grained steels tend to exhibit a longer fatigue initiation
phase for dislocations [29].
2.2.3
Crack Propagation
The phase of crack propagation starts when the microscopic crack has progressed to a detectable
macroscopic crack. The size from when a crack transitions from micro to macro is not unanimously
agreed upon, but is generally said to be in the region of 0 5-1 mm [30]. The cracks change angle and
15
==PAGE:48==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
subsequently start to propagate on a plane perpendicular to the maximum principal stresses [26].
In this region, there is a log linear relation between the crack growth rate (cgr) and the applied
variation of crack driving force (∆).
The main crack driving force for structures is often the
stress range, ∆σ, which is deﬁned as the diﬀerence between the maximum and minimum principal
stresses, perpendicular to the crack plane. The crack propagation region is also often denoted as
the region with stable crack growth.
2.2.4
Structural Failure
The phase of structural failure, the shortest of the three, commences when the crack has propagated
far enough through the structural member to cause failure. Several failure mechanisms can entail,
depending on the geometry, stresses and environmental conditions. Expressions have been derived
to describe crack growth in this region but for the case of oﬀshore structures are made redundant
due to the impending structural failure [31].
2.3
S-N Curve Approach
Fatigue is characterised by a gradual reduction in the capacity of the material to withstand cyclic
loads, caused by crack propagation. The second phase of the fatigue development; the crack prop-
agation phase, is the phase that is conventionally described with a fatigue strength model [32].
Assessment of the fatigue strength of a structural component by the S-N approach introduces
the damage indicator, D. The damage will be considered as zero prior to the ﬁrst load cycle, and
unity at failure. Thus the failure criterion for material fatigue under repeated loading is D = 1.
The S-N curve approach is based on quantifying the fatigue behaviour of a structural detail,
by conducting fatigue experiments on a number of identical specimens, where the failure criteria
inherent in the S-N curves is normally given as a surface crack, which has propagated completely
through the member, leading to failure [26] [31]. The specimens are subject to constant amplitude
loading (CAL) [26].
Figure 2.10: The principle of S-N curves for one type of test specimen
16
==PAGE:49==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
The loading is characterised by the diﬀerence between the maximum nominal stress σmax and
the minimum stress σmin, denoted as the stress range, S, as seen on the left of Figure 2.10.
Diﬀerent constant amplitude stress ranges are applied to the diﬀerent specimens and the number
of cycles until failure, N is recorded. A scatter plot of the stress range, S, against number of
cycles to failure, N, is plotted, as presented in the right plot of Figure 2.10. Fitting the empirically
collected data to a best ﬁt log-linear curve, yields a very simple relation between applied stress
range, and number of cycles until failure.
The best ﬁt curve shown in Figure 2.10, also denoted as the mean curve, yields 50% probability
of failure (PoF), which from a design point of view is undesirable. To introduce a greater level
of safety, most norms prescribing S-N curve fatigue design procedures employ characteristic S-N
curves. As seen in Figure 2.11, the mean curve is shifted by two standard deviations, reducing
mean values by approximately 25% and in turn ensures a signiﬁcantly lower PoF of 2.3% [27].
Figure 2.11: Sketch of mean and characteristic S-N curves
An oﬀshore marine structure will experience a dynamic load history of a stochastic nature, as
opposed to CAL. Fatigue damage developed under stochastic or random dynamic loading is de-
noted the cumulative damage. There are various methods of calculating cumulative damage from
S-N data with the most accepted and applied method being the Palmgren Miner’s summation.
2.3.1
Cumulative Damage Method
The principle behind the Palmgren Miner’s summation is that the damage done pr. load cycle, is
assumed to be constant at a given stress range. This allows for the linear summation of damages
from variable amplitude loading (VAL), presenting a simple way of determining the accumulated
damage:
Dtot =
n
X

Where, ni is the number of cycles experienced at a nominal stress range ∆σi, and Ni the num-

==PAGE:50==
2. Fundamental Theory of Conventional Procedures
ber of cycles to failure for ∆σi, based on S-N data.
For a structure subject to variable loading, the fatigue life is assessed by determining the total
fatigue damage by summing damages calculated for discrete stress components within the load
spectrum. A manageable way of presenting the discrete fatigue load data is by a Markov matrix.
The principle of a Markov matrix is seen in Table 2.1, where ∆σ is the stress range and ¯σ is the
mean stress. Each bin in the table then corresponds to the number of cycles ni,i occurring for each
combination of ∆σi and ¯σi.

Figure 2.12 depicts an example of a random stress time series. To convert the stress history
into a manageable set of stress ranges, a count of the stress ranges and their respective number of
occurrences must be performed, typically by rain ﬂow counting.
Figure 2.12: Example of a stress history and applied rain ﬂow counting
Figure 2.12 illustrates the procedure of rain ﬂow counting performed on the stress time series,
which will not be explained in detail. The rain ﬂow count yields a spectrum of stress ranges and
the respective number of occurrences. In order to manage the data produced by the rain ﬂow
counting, a band width of the stress ranges must be determined. A Narrower band width results
in a larger number of discrete stress ranges, and consequently more calculations to be performed,
but also a more precise representation of the actual stress history. The discretisation of data yields
stress ranges and corresponding number of occurrences, as represented in Figure 2.13a. The S-N
curve can be applied, as seen in Figure 2.13b, to determine the number of cycles until failure, N,
for each stress range.

Figure 2.13: Discretisation of ∆σi and Ni determined for each range

2.3.2
Damage Equivalent Loads Method
Alternatively to the cumulative damage method, one can apply the damage equivalent load (DEL)
method. The principle is to calculate a single representative load, which can be applied to the S-N
curves to determine the damage - hence the name. The starting point is the same as the cumulative
damage method; a discrete data set of stress ranges and corresponding number of occurrences. The
damage equivalent load is then determined by:
Feq =
Pn

Where,

Fi and Ni

Nref

is the Wöhler curve exponent
The reference number of cycles Nref is chosen by the designer and will be the number of cycles
to apply in the S-N curve calculation together with the damage equivalent load. Typically the
reference number of cycles is set to be Nref = 1 · 107 [16].

by Palmgren-miner’s expression as follows, by determining the cycles to failure from ∆σeq as in
Figure 2.14:
D = Nref
Neq
(2.4)
For a log-linear S-N curve with a constant slope, the damage equivalent load method will
correspond exactly to the cumulative damage method. For the bi log-linear S-N curves where
there are two slopes, it is possible that the two methods will not yield exactly the same damage.
This is due to the simpliﬁcation of the DEL method, where only one Wöhler’s exponent is applied
for all stress bins. Typically the Wöhler’s exponent used in the DEL method is chosen to be the
most critical of the two, and therefore the DEL method will yield a conservative damage.
20
==PAGE:53==
3
Literature and Norms for
Conventional Procedures
Fatigue design of monopiles is generally a challenging feat due to the corrosive environment and
the prominent dynamic actions. Therefore, an extensive assessment should be performed taking
all additional inﬂuences introduced by the environment into account, to ensure an optimal design
with suﬃcient capacity.
In the following section a description of the procedures prescribed in
DNVGL-RP-C203 will be presented.
3.1
DNVGL-RP-C203: Fatigue Design of Oﬀshore Steel
Structures
DNVGL-RP-C203 presents recommended practices for fatigue analysis of oﬀshore steel structures
mainly by use of the S-N approach. To ensure structural integrity, fatigue analysis must be per-
formed for each structural detail subjected to fatigue loading. The methodology is valid for fatigue
crack growth from: a weld toe into base material, the weld root through a ﬁllet weld, the weld root
into the HAZ and surface irregularities or notches. As this thesis concerns double-sided FPBWs,
the ﬁrst failure mechanism is the main focus. Therefore only procedures relevant for FPBWs is
included in the following.
3.1.1
S-N curve Expression
The standard S-N curve is given as follows:
log(N) = log(¯a) −m log(∆σ)
(3.1)
Where,
N
is the number of cycles to failure [−]
log(¯a)
is the intercept of the S-N curve with the log N-axis [−]
m
is the negative inverse slope of the S-N curve [−]
∆σ
is the stress range [MPa]
The fatigue strength is dependent on the thickness of the plate in which the crack will propagate
through. Expression (3.1) is modiﬁed for cases where the thickness of the member exceeds 25 mm.
This is due to the fact that the initial tests performed to develop the S-N curves were performed
on specimens with limited plate thicknesses. Further investigations have shown that increasing the
21
==PAGE:54==
3. Literature and Norms for Conventional Procedures
plate thickness while, retaining all other parameters, reduces the fatigue life of the detail. The ﬁrst
reason for the reduction is the statistical eﬀect, meaning an increase in plate thickness and weld
size increases the risk of imperfections and stress notches.
Figure 3.1: Thickness eﬀect illustration showing a larger stress at the crack front for the thicker
plate
The second reason is the change in stress gradient as a function of the thickness. Figure 3.1
shows how two plates, with thicknesses t1 and t2 and the same initial ﬂaw depth, ai, have diﬀerent
peak stresses at the crack front, σ1 and σ2. The higher peak stress, combined with a more moderate
slope leads to a greater crack growth rate (CGR) and in turn a shorter fatigue life as presented
by Gurney [26]. The thickness modiﬁcation originally proposed by T. Gurney, yields the following
expression:
log(N) = log(¯a) −m log
 
∆σ
teff
tref
k!
teff ≥tref
(3.2)
Where,
teff
is the eﬀective thickness of the member (Expression 3.3) [mm]
tref
is the reference thickness of 25 [mm]
k
is the detail category speciﬁc thickness exponent [−]
Apart from the thickness of the plate, the eﬀective width of the joint also has an eﬀect. A
modiﬁcation can be made to the thickness applied, teff, in Expression (3.2) as follows, where
the Lt is deﬁned in Figure 3.2. The expression is based on studies performed by Lotsberg [28],
investigating the stiﬀness inﬂuence of welds and attachments on the fatigue life.
teff = min((14 mm + 0.66Lt), t)
(3.3)
22
==PAGE:55==
3. Literature and Norms for Conventional Procedures

The ﬁnal modiﬁcation to the expression, is the correction to the nominal stress for any stress
concentrations which may arise, which are not inherent in the basic S-N curves, such as misalign-
ment or changes in thickness at welded joints. The ﬁnal expression for the S-N curve according to
C203 is as follows:
log(N) = log(¯a) −m log

log(¯a) and m depend on the structural detail in question as well as the environment the structure
is located in.
DNVGL-RP-C203 prescribes a categorisation system for structural details. Double-sided FPBWs
are classiﬁed as D-curves, where the failure mode is expected to be an edge crack, initiating from the
weld toe [33]. Figure 3.3 illustrates the geometric assembly for D curves for tubular joints. These
curves are also applicable for joints between sections with varying thicknesses or conical/cylindrical
transitions, if the additional SCFs are considered.
Figure 3.3: Illustration of category D for tubular connections
DNVGL-RP-C203 distinguishes between 3 diﬀerent environments: air, seawater with cathodic
protection and seawater with free corrosion. For a 100mm thick plate, a D curve is plotted in Figure

==PAGE:56==

3.4 for the three options. The number of cycles to failure has been highlighted for an example of
∆σ = 100 MPa, showing approximately 600,000, 250,000 and 200,000 for air, protected and free
respectively, emphasising the importance of environmental conditions.
Figure 3.4: Example of D curve for 100mm plate for diﬀerent environments according to
DNVGL-RP-C203
3.1.2
Stress Concentrations Factors
The stress range in Expression (3.4) must take any additional stress concentration factors (SCFs)
into account which are not already inherent to the curves.
For tubular connections, such as
monopile circumferential welds, these additional SCFs can arise from a number of issues such as:
misalignment (axially misaligned can sections), ovality (out-of-roundness of one or both cans) or
thickness jumps (diﬀerence in diameters).
The ovality and misalignment contributions are merged into one, δm, describing the total mis-
alignment between to adjacent surfaces. The thickness contribution, δt takes the change of neutral
axis between two cans into account. Figure 3.5 illustrates the two contributions as well as other
parameters which will be explained in the following paragraphs.
Figure 3.5: Illustration of internal and external SCF contributions for doubled-sided butt welds,
including both thickness and fabrication misalignments
Figure 3.5 shows two plates welded together by a doubled-sided FPBW. The varying thickness
leads to a shift in the neutral axis, which gives rise to a contribution to a stress concentration,
24
==PAGE:57==
3. Literature and Norms for Conventional Procedures
determined as follows:
δt = 1
2(T −t)
(3.5)
When adjoining two plates of diﬀerent thicknesses, bevelling is necessary to avoid a sharp
transition.The length of the sloped transition, is given by Expression (3.6), where a is the inverse
slope, which should be no less than 4 for fatigue loaded butt weld joints [34].
L = (T −t) · a
(3.6)
Furthermore the ﬁgure depicts a fabrication misalignment between the two plates, δm. The
misalignment between the external surfaces can range from 0.1t-0.3t, depending on the structural
category although a max value of 4 mm is an MP fabrication requirement [34]. δ0 is a correction
factor which accounts for the misalignment embedded in the S-N curves. The value of δ0 varies
according to the weld detail, equalling 0.05t for as-welded and 0 for ground welds. Finally, the
SCFs for the inside and outside of the joint can be seen in the following expressions, respectively.
SCFint = 1 + 6(δt + δm −δ0)

3.1.3
Design Fatigue Factor
The Design Fatigue Factor (DFF) is applied to the characteristic damage determined from the
Palmgren-Miner summation. The DFF depends on the target probability of failure, as well as
planned inspection intervals, and is applied as a factor to the fatigue damage D. Thus, a higher
DFF results in more conservatism.
The DFF will usually assume a value of either 1, 2 or 3, depending on the inspection plan and
accessibility of a detail [35] [36]. The inspection intervals are a function of the DFF and given as
follows:
Inspection Interval = Design Fatigue Life · DFF
3.0
(3.10)
A DFF of 3 is usually applied to locations which are inaccessible due being below the seabed
or in locations which require specialised, expensive equipment to reach. A full explanation of the
guides and requirements of DFFs for various locations are shown in Appendix A.2: DNVGL-ST-
0126 (Table 4-20).
25
==PAGE:58==
3. Literature and Norms for Conventional Procedures
The DFF impacts the accumulated probability of failure, reducing the PoF for increasing values
of DFF. Although there is no generic way to establish a relation between the PoF and the DFF
and further determining an exact PoF is no trivial feat, studies have been performed which give
an indication of the order of magnitude.
An investigation performed by Velarde et al. [37] considering fatigue reliability of MP struc-
tures gives an insight into statistical parameters which can be used to determine the PoF for a
DFF = 3. Combining the parameters with investigations performed by Lotsberg et al. [38] it can
be deduced that a DFF = 3 results in a PoF ≈0.1% for a 20 year design life.
Similarly, analyses performed by Wang et al.
showed that for a 20 year design life with a
D-curve, resulted in a PoF ≈0.2%. DNVGL-RP-C203 also gives guidance on the relation between
PoF and DFF. With varying statistical parameters, similar to ones mentioned previously for a
20-year design period, a DFF=3 yields a PoF = 0.2-1%. A DFF of 3.8 - 5.5 would yield a PoF =
0.1% for a 20 year design life, depending on statistical uncertainties.
3.2
Norm Procedure of Brittle Fracture Assessment
DNVGL-ST-0126 [35] and EN 1993-1-10 [39] both present procedures for ensuring a structural
component against brittle fracture.
In DNVGL-ST-0126, the maximum allowable thickness is
determined based on three parameters; the structural category, the steel grade and the service
temperature. In EN 1993-1-10, the allowable thickness is likewise determined based three param-
eters; the proportional stress level, the steel grade and the service temperature.
An in depth background study of the basis of the tabulated values of the brittle fracture
assessment of EN 1993-1-10 has been performed by the authors, prior to this study, Fracture
Mechanical Background Investigation of Steel Element Thickness in Eurocode 1993-1-10 [19]. For
a detailed description of the procedure, as well as complete understanding of the fracture mechanical
calculations making the basis of the method, reference is made to this study. In the following, the
methodology of both EN 1993-1-10 and ST-0126 will be introduced brieﬂy.
3.2.1
EN 1993-1-10
The assessment of brittle fracture is a relation between member thickness and service temperature.
A table of maximum allowable member thicknesses has been developed, of which a selected part
is presented in Table 3.1. The procedure of verifying suﬃcient capacity against brittle fracture
consists of determining the steel grade, the stress state, the reference temperature and ﬁnally the
max allowable member thickness by table lookup.
Brittle fracture is categorised as an accidental limit state (ALS). Hence the load combinations
to be considered are generally similar to the ULS load cases, but without partial safety factors.
26
==PAGE:59==
3. Literature and Norms for Conventional Procedures
Based on the assumption that the ULS capacity is fully utilised, the ALS stress will not exceed
75% of the yield stress, hence the reference stress of Table 3.1 maxes at σEd = 0.75fy(t).
Table 3.1: Maximum permissible element thickness values for S355 (Part of Table 2.1 [39])
The reference temperature TEd, of the speciﬁc case must be determined, and consists of contri-
butions from the air temperature, radiation loss, strain rate, cold forming and the crack/member
shape as well as the stress state. Appendix A.4: EN 1993-1-10 (Table 2.1) covers the temperature
contributions and reference is made to the previous study for in-depth explanations [19]. For a
number of countries, accompanying national annexes (NAs) to EN 1993-1-10 have been developed,
to further assist in determining the contributions.
Counter-intuitively, diﬀerent countries have
diﬀerent ways of taking the crack/member shape and stress state into account as highlighted in
the prior study [19].
EN 1993-1-10 allows for use of fracture mechanics for analyses of speciﬁc geometries, as an
alternative to the tabulated values. Though no further guidelines or references to relevant fracture
mechanical methodology is presented in the norm.
3.2.2
DNVGL-ST-0126
Table 3.2 shows a cut-out of Table 4-4 of DNVGL-ST-0126.
The table allows the designer to
select a member category (complexity of the stress state and importance of the member), strength
group of a member (yield strength), a Charpy-V test temperature and the in-service temperature
of the steel member to determine the maximum allowable wall thickness. The full table is shown in
Appendix A.5: DNVGL-ST-0126 (Table 4-4), along with a more detailed description of the design
parameters.

4
Monopile Assessment by
Conventional Procedures
The structural basis of this thesis consists of detailed information of an ongoing undisclosed oﬀshore
wind farm project, courtesy of COWI A/S. The supporting structure of the wind turbines consists
of a Monopile (MP) - Transition Piece (TP) assembly, sketched in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Illustration of assembled WTG structure
29
==PAGE:62==
4. Monopile Assessment by Conventional Procedures
4.1
General Design Requirements
The MP is designed for an initial installation/commissioning phase of 1.25 years (15 months),
followed by 30 years of standard operation and ﬁnally 0.75 years (9 months) of decommissioning,
a total of 32 years. The design of the substructure follows a speciﬁc norm hierarchy, ranked in
the following order; DNVGL, IEC, EN, ISO/NORSOK, additional literature. A more detailed
overview of the norm hierarchy is shown in Appendix E.1: Detailed Norm Hierarchy.
4.2
Overview of Structural Basis
Four documents have been provided by COWI A/S, consisting of the Design Basis A [40], Design
Basis B [41], Integrated Load Analysis 1 (ILA1) [42] and an Input Report [16]. As mentioned
previously, this thesis will focus solely on circumferential butt welds located in critical sections.
The critical weld has been determined based on the FLS results of Input Report, where weld nr.
10, between cans 10 and 11 proved to be the highest utilised weld. Therefore, only information
relevant to this detail will be addressed and summarised in the following sections.
4.2.1
Geometry
A sketch of the MP including dimensions is shown in Figure 4.2. The detailed MP geometry can
be seen in Appendix E.2: Primary Steel Geometry. The MP consists of 16 cans, with a total height
of 55.738m and a constant outer diameter of 7000mm. Cans 10 and 11 both have nominal wall
thicknesses of 78 mm.
Figure 4.2: Sketch of MP, with can numbering and dimensions
4.2.2
Materials
All material properties and categorisations are established in accordance to DNVGL-ST-0126.
The mechanical material properties for 78 mm S355ML/NL steel are given as σy = 325 MPa,
σu = 470 MPa, E = 210 MPa and ν = 0.3. Monopile cans and welds are categorised as Primary
structural components, due to their importance regarding the structural integrity. The Charpy-V
impact energy requirements for Primary components of S355 NL/ML steel are given as 50 J and
30
==PAGE:63==
4. Monopile Assessment by Conventional Procedures
34 J for the longitudinal and transverse directions respectively, at a testing temperature of -60◦C.
4.2.3
Loads
COWI A/S have supplied foundation loads from an Integrated Load Analysis (ILA) [42]. The ILA
supplies detailed fatigue load data, for axial, bending, shear and torsional actions. The stresses
due to the ﬂuctuating bending moments and axial loads are prominent and will be the main focus,
i.e.
the torsional moments and shear forces will be disregarded in the analyses of this thesis.
Furthermore a design fatigue factor of DFF = 3.0 will be applied to the calculated damages of the
entire structure. The implication of the DFF will be elaborated further in Section 3.1.3.
4.2.3.1
In-Service Loads
The loads from the supplied Markov matrices have been converted to damage equivalent loads
(DELs), elaborated in Section 2.3.2, for FLS, Based on an averaged commissioning and decommis-
sioning time in-service fatigue loads have been supplied for 31 years. The maximum ULS loads
expected during the life time have been supplied for each elevation. Table 4.1 shows the FLS and
ULS envelope loads for weld nr. 10.

4.2.3.2
Installation Loads
Prior to the in-service phase, the MP is transported from the docks to the location of installation
and subsequently driven into the seabed. The MP is driven into the seabed by application of
a large impact force to the top, subsequently forcing the MP into the ground. The pile-driving
consists of 2000 hammer blows, resulting in a stress range of ∆σ = 123 MPa at the location of
weld nr. 10. The combined T&I damages are assumed to reach D =0.069 in the input report,
with 0.019 attributed to pile-driving and 0.05 to transportation. Since no stresses are given for
the transport phase, it will only be considered for the analysis to verify input results and omitted
from the fracture mechanical analysis.
4.2.4
Environmental Conditions
The Design Air Temperature, deﬁned as the lowest daily averaged air temperature, is given as
Tmd = −4.9◦C. DNVGL-ST-0126 states that material located below the lowest astronomical tide
(LAT) may be designed for a service temperature of Tmd = 0◦C [35].
The structure will be protected against corrosion in critical areas by both protective coating and
cathodic protection systems. The corrosion allowance (CA) for cans 10 and 11 in the submerged
31
==PAGE:64==
4. Monopile Assessment by Conventional Procedures
zone is 0.5 mm on the internal surface and 0 mm for the external surface. The size and location of
the corrosion zones are illustrated in Appendix E.3: MP Corrosion Zones. The expected protection
potential of the cathodic system has been supplied by COWI, at both the time of commissioning
and at the end of the design life, shown in Appendix E.4 External GACP Cathodic Protection
Potential.
4.3
Current Structural Assessment Practices Applied
In the following section, both the fatigue and the brittle fracture design procedures presented in the
previous sections will be applied to the critical weld of the structural basis - determining the fatigue
life and assessing the risk of brittle fracture by the approach currently applied in the industry.
4.3.1

focusing on weld nr. 10. In-detail results for all welds are shown in Appendix F.1: External MP
FLS Check . The relevant S-N curve for the weld nr. 10 of the monopile is a D-curve for corrosion
protected members.
The curve is deﬁned by an inverse slope of m = 5, with an intercept of

5
Fundamental Theory of Fracture
Mechanics
The ﬁeld of fracture mechanics diﬀerentiates from the conventional strength of materials approach
by not only analysing the stress state and yield/ultimate tensile strength of the material, but
assesses ﬂaws, the mechanical properties and loading conditions under speciﬁc circumstances. This
section will cover the introduction of fracture mechanics and how they can be applied to describe
fatigue damage accumulation as well as risk of rupture. When applying fracture mechanics, there
are two primary groups, Linear-Elastic (LEFM) and Elastic-Plastic (EPFM). Through out this
thesis, the focus is primarily on LEFM and argumentation for its suﬃciency is made.
5.1
Linear-Elastic Fracture Mechanics
Three primary cracking modes can be observed when loading a structural component. The three
modes are illustrated in Figure 5.1. Cracks are not limited to being loading by a single mode at a
time but can be subject to mixed mode loading, where the crack driving force excites more than
one cracking mode. Mixed mode loading will not be covered in this thesis.
Mode I, the opening mode, where the crack driving force acts perpendicular to the crack plane.
Mode I is generally the predominant failure mode and will also be the main focus of this thesis.
Mode II, the sliding mode, where the crack driving force acts parallel in-plane to the crack face
as an in-plane shear force.
Mode III, the tearing mode, where the crack driving force acts parallel to the crack plane, opening
the crack through an out-of-plane shear force.
Figure 5.1: Illustration of the three crack opening modes
35
==PAGE:68==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
5.1.1

nitude of the diﬀerent stress components can be determined from Expressions (5.1-5.3).

LEFM builds on the principle of similitude meaning the crack tip zone can be described by a
single parameter, such as the stress intensity factor, K. The stress intensity factor, K, is a well
deﬁned parameter for a vast range of crack sizes and shapes. The base case on which all solutions
are built, is for a through thickness crack in a inﬁnitely wide plate, as shown in Figure 5.3 and in
Expression (5.5).

factor, Mk, which takes any external geometry and stress concentrations into account, such as weld
beads. Therefore, a more general expression for KI is given as:
K1 = σ√πa Y Mk

5.1.2
Variable Stress Distributions
Structures are seldom subject to a constant stress through the thickness and more often variable
stress distributions. Stress variations through the thickness can arise from fabrication and welding
residual stresses, stress concentrations or local bending.
A simple method of accounting for variable through-thickness stress, is the application of in-
ﬂuence coeﬃcients. This approach is applicable if the through-thickness stress distribution can be
expressed or approximated by a polynomial. Given a plate with a surface crack as seen in Figure
5.4 and a 3rd order polynomial stress distribution as per Expression (5.7) a representative SIF can
be determined [20].

Where, ii are the inﬂuence coeﬃcients applied to the stresses, to obtain an accurate SIF.
For more complex loading scenarios where the stresses cannot be approximated by polynomials
with suﬃcient accuracy, weight functions can be applied. Weight functions allow for arbitrary
loads to be applied if they can be expressed through continuous functions.

Where, h(z) is deﬁned as the stress intensity from applying a unit stress at the crack tip, at
z and σ(z) is the stress distribution as function of the depth z. The weight function depends on
only the geometry of the cracked body. Given the weight function for a particular conﬁguration,
it is possible to determine the stress intensity for any arbitrary loading. Weight functions can be
applied for 2D as well as 3D problems, given that the loading conﬁguration over the cracked body
can be represented by a function [20].
For crack propagation analyses, weight functions methods can signiﬁcantly increase computa-
tional times as the expression of the weight functions will change for each increment.
5.1.3
Limitations of LEFM
One of the main limitations of LEFM, is the assumption of none or small scale yielding (SSY).
The assumption of SSY implies the crack growth and fracture is characterised by linear-elastic
conditions, an assumption that signiﬁcantly reduces the complexity of an analysis. A common
method of determining the limit of LEFM, is commonly referred to as the ASTM small-scale-
yielding criterion as shown below [43]:
a > 2.5K2
I
σ2y
(5.10)
The relation is visualised for S235, S355 and S420 steels in Figure 5.5, showing the maximum
allowable value of K1 for a given crack depth.
Figure 5.5: Maximum allowable K1 for a given crack depth for SSY for various structural steels
38
==PAGE:71==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
The relation can be re-written as shown in Equation (5.11), to deﬁne the limits of small scale
yielding as a function of the applied stress for the base case in Figure 5.3.

Therefore, if the applied stress is below 0.36σy, the assumption of SSY is applicable for the
inﬁnitely wide plate in Figure 5.3, allowing for use of LEFM. Additionally, Anderson explicitly
states that when the applied stresses exceeds 50% of the yield strength of the material, LEFM
should be used with caution and EPFM should instead be implemented [20].
5.2
Elastic-Plastic Fracture Mechanics
When entering the domain of elastic-plastic fracture mechanics, new parameters are introduced
to describe the crack tip conditions. The two parameters are the crack tip opening displacement
(CTOD) and J contour integral, of which the J integral will be brieﬂy introduced.
Figure 5.6: Loading and unloading behaviour of non-linear elastic and elastic plastic materials
The J-integral is applicable to cases with monotonically increased loading, for modelling of the
material as non-linear elastic. Non-linear elasticity implies that the material exhibits an elastic-
plastic behaviour during load application, but retracts completely to the initial state after unload-
ing, i.e. no permanent plasticity. This contrasts elastic-plastic behaviour, where any exceedance of
the linear region will result in irreversible plasticity, and after unloading the material display per-
manent changes to the initial state. Figure 5.6 illustrates the stress-strain curves for a non-linear
elastic material and an elastic-plastic material [20].
The J-integral serves as an alternative parameter describing the stress state in front of the
crack. If need be, J can be converted into an equivalent of K by following relation:
J = K2
I
E′
⇒

JE′
(5.12)
Where

5.3
Justiﬁcation of Linear-Elastic Fracture Mechanics
In general, when moving away from LEFM to EPFM, the complexity greatly increases, therefore
several correction factors and adjustments can be made to LEFM to avoid EPFM.
When utilising LEFM, a correction factor taking the crack tip plasticity into account can be
applied. The correction factor is based upon Irwin’s ﬁrst order approximation of the plastic zone
and on a plane stress assumption. The ﬁrst order approximation is not physically feasible, as it
does not take the redistribution of stress into account. By performing a force balance, a second
order approximation of the plastic zone, rp, can be determined which is twice the size of the ﬁrst
order, ry [20].
ry,stress = 1

with the new expression, the second order approximation for plain strain equals approximately a
40% smaller plastic zone as compared to the plain stress case.

Figure 5.7 illustrates the plastic zones for the ﬁrst and second order approximations for the
case of plane strain. The second order approximation shows a substantially larger plastic zone,
due to the inclusion of plasticity as opposed to a linear-elastic assumption.
A comparison of standard LEFM, Irwin correction and Strip yield methods, can be seen in
Figure 5.8 for a through thickness crack in plane strain [20]. The Figure shows divergence between
the methods occurs at approximately 40% of the yield strength, corresponding to the re-writing of
the ASTM Small Scale Yielding limit.
40
==PAGE:73==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
Figure 5.8: Comparison of plastic zone corrections for a though thickness crack in plane strain
Additionally, during cyclic loading the plastic zone is generally accepted as approximately a
quarter the size of monotonic static loading. This is due to the fact the plastic zone has more
time to develop and expand during monotonic loading, as opposed to the ﬂuctuating loads during
fatigue loading [26].
In summary the following conditions justify the use of LEFM: in general high cycle fatigue
loads are well below the yield strength of the material, reducing the risk of large plastic zones.
Furthermore, the thickness of the plates practised for monopiles allows for signiﬁcant triaxiality
near the crack font, once again reducing the size of the plastic zones. Finally high-cycle fatigue
does not generate plastic zones of the same magnitude as monotonic and quasi-static loading.
Accordingly, LEFM is considered representative for the majority of fatigue analyses. Furthermore,
when assessing the ﬁnal fracture, the failure assessment diagram method allows for consideration
of plastic eﬀects further increasing the accuracy of LEFM at higher loads, which will be further
elaborated in Section 6.1.
5.4
Crack Propagation
Fracture mechanical theory allows for modelling of crack propagation. As mentioned in Section
2.2.1, fatigue can be split into 3 categories: crack initiation, crack propagation and failure. These
three phases are illustrated in Figure 5.9 as Region I, Region II and Region III. Several crack
growth laws have been developed to describe either one or multiple regions, with Region II being
of most interest.
The crack propagation during cyclic loading under linear-elastic conditions will only vary with
the stress intensity factor, upholding the requirements of similitude. ∆K, and the R-ratio, are a
function of K, ∆K = Kmax −Kmin and R = Kmin/Kmax. This allows for the formulation of a
crack propagation formula of the following form:
da
dN = f(∆K, R)
(5.16)
41
==PAGE:74==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
Figure 5.9: Illustration of the three regions of the Paris-Erdogan Law
The most common linear-elastic crack growth formula for Region II is known as the Paris-
Erdogan law which follows the principle of similitude. The expression is given as follows, where C
and m are material speciﬁc crack growth parameters [44]:
da
dN = C(∆K)m
∆Kth < ∆K < Kc
(5.17)
The material constants C, m and ∆Kth are heavily dependent on the material, R-ratio and
environmental conditions of the member, which will be elaborated further in Section 5.4.1. The
simple Paris-Erdogan law has proven to be an accurate descriptor of crack growth and will there-
fore form the basis of this thesis. Nevertheless, the remainder of this section will introduce more
advanced crack growth laws to enlighten the possibilities for even more accurate modelling.
The basic equation by Paris and Erdogan has since been expanded by Klesnil and Lukas [45],
to take the threshold value into account, ∆Kth, thereby accounting for both Region I and Region
II. The expanded formula, as shown in Expression (5.18), reduces to Expression (5.17) when the
values of ∆K result in
da
dN > 10−6 mm
cycle.
da
dN = C(∆Km −∆Km
th)
(5.18)
Another expansion was proposed by Forman and Mettu [46], attempting to describe crack
growth in Regions I, II and III, where C, m, p and q are material speciﬁc crack growth parameters.
42
==PAGE:75==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
The version presented in Expression 5.19 does not take crack closure into account.
da
dN = C∆Km
