(5.19)
When moving from constant amplitude cyclic loading to variable amplitude loading, the simili-
tude assumption can in some cases become invalid. If the variable amplitude loading has signiﬁcant
peaks and troughs, i.e. overloads and underloads which can cause blunting or retardation of the
crack, the load history becomes a variable. Thereby the crack propagation becomes a function of
both K, R and the load history, H.
da
dN = f(∆K, R, H)
(5.20)
Usually for structures where high R-ratios are present, such as welded steel structures, crack
closure does not occur, meaning the assumption of similitude can still be viable. If similitude is
still viable, the damage can be approximated by linear damage summation, where each the damage
from each stress range can be summed to determine the total damage. If the range of the VAL
is large and signiﬁcant overloads are present, crack retardation is not taken into account and can
therefore be conservative [20]
5.4.1
Factors Inﬂuencing Crack Growth Parameters
The crack growth formulations presented all depend on material speciﬁc parameters, which vary
under diﬀerent conditions, which will be elaborated in the following.
5.4.1.1
Material Properties
Materials with a larger Young’s modulus, E, also tend to have a slightly larger ∆Kth and lower crack
growth rate [30]. The crack growth rate can further be reduced by heat treating the material, with
the greatest inﬂuence being achieved for regions I and III. Fine grained steels, such as ML/NL
steels with high weldability, tend to exhibit a higher sensitivity to notches due to higher stress
concentrations, which in turn leads to a lower ∆Kth as opposed to coarser grained steels [47] [29].
5.4.1.2
Environment and Temperature
In Region II the dependency of R dissipates when exceeding R = 0.4, for cases where corrosion
is absent. For oﬀshore structures, subject to corrosion or with cathodic protection, this limit is
increased to R = 0.5 [30] [48], with other studies reporting the limit reaching R = 0.7, substantially
increasing the crack growth rate [26].
Additionally, studies have shown a temperature dependency for the crack growth rate and
threshold value. An increase in temperature increases the threshold value and reduces crack growth
near the threshold value, in region I. When approaching and entering region II, increased temper-
43
==PAGE:76==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
atures generate higher crack growth rates which is further ampliﬁed in corrosive environments
[26].
5.4.1.3
Loading Conditions
The stress intensity factor threshold value, ∆Kth, is also known to vary in the case of constant
amplitude cyclic loading with R, decreasing for increasing R values [26]. Furthermore, in cases of
high frequency loading, the threshold value and crack growth rates near the threshold limit can be
aﬀected to due an increase in temperature.
5.5
Fracture Mechanisms in Steel
This section will bridge the introduced fracture mechanical theory to steel structures, introducing
the fracture toughness as well as ductile and brittle fracture. The failure of a structural member
ensues once a critical ﬂaw geometry has been reached, i.e. a critical combination of a and l. The
critical crack dimensions depend on the geometry of the member as well as the stress state and
the temperature, which dictate the fracture toughness of the material.
5.5.1
Fracture Toughness
The fracture toughness, Kmat, also denoted Kc, is a measure of the ductility of a material. Figure
5.10 depicts the inﬂuence of fracture toughness on the failure mode.
Steels with low fracture
toughnesses tend to fail due to brittle fracture. A greater fracture toughness will reduce the risk of
brittle fracture, resulting in a more ductile material behaviour when approaching failure, exhibiting
plastic deformation and ﬁnally collapse.
Figure 5.10: Eﬀect of fracture toughness on the governing failure mechanism
The fracture toughness is obtained by testing. A simple way of quantifying the fracture tough-
ness of a material is a correlation to the Charpy-V absorbed impact energy of a material. Here
an increase in absorbed energy in turn also means a greater fracture toughness. Although the
Charpy-V test does not directly yield a value for the fracture toughness, the test results can be
used to estimate Kmat.
44
==PAGE:77==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
More exact tests can be performed to obtain a more precise estimate of the fracture toughness.
The tests are generally performed with pre-cracked specimens. There are many types of fracture
toughness test specimens, originally introduced by ASTM, such as compact tension (C(T)), single-
edge notch bend (SE(B)) or middle tension (MT) amongst others. The specimens are subject to
Mode I loading, in a load controlled test, where the applied load at which unstable crack growth is
monitored. Generally speaking, the fracture toughness is quantiﬁed by a single parameter, Kmat,
but can also be characterised by a so called crack growth resistance curve (K-R curve), where Kmat
becomes a function of the crack size.
Crack tip opening displacement (CTOD) testing is another way in which the fracture toughness
can be quantiﬁed. Similar to the previously mentioned tests, a specimen of standardised dimensions
is subject to a tensile load. The crack opening is monitored as an additional variable during the
test. This allows for the fracture toughness to be measured as a function of both loading and crack
tip opening [49].
5.5.1.1
Factors Inﬂuencing the Fracture Toughness
Several factors inﬂuence the fracture toughness of a material and thereby the ductile-brittle frac-
ture dependencies.
The thickness of a member greatly aﬀects Kmat, where an increasing thickness reduces the
toughness. This phenomenon is attributed to an increasing fraction of the fracture surface failing
due to plane fracture as opposed to shear fracture. For increasing plate thicknesses, a larger fraction
of the fracture surface can be described by a tri-axial stress state, causing a ﬂat fracture surface.
Figure 5.11a depicts how an increasing thickness result in a greater ﬂat/shear ratio and Figure
5.11b depicting the state of triaxility from the centreline of the plate.
(a) Shear/ﬂat fracture surface ratio
(b) Stress triaxility over the thickness
Figure 5.11: Eﬀect of thickness on shear/ﬂat fracture surface ratio
Similarly to Charpy impact energies, the toughness also exhibits a temperature dependency,
45
==PAGE:78==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
where decreasing temperatures signiﬁcantly reduce the toughness.
Ferritic steels, which make
up the majority of structural steels, with a body-centred cubic crystal structure show a strong
dependency on the temperature.
Figure 5.12 shows a sketch of the transition from ductile to
brittle with a decreasing temperature.
Figure 5.12: Ductile/brittle temperature dependency for Charpy-V absorbed energy
Steels also display an inverse relation between toughness and strength, where an increase in
yield strength of a material reduces the fracture toughness although ﬁne grained steels, exhibit
both increased strength and toughness [29]. The strain rate, or rate of loading, also has an eﬀect.
Large strain rates, which can follow from impact loading show an increased tendency of brittle
fracture [26] [50] .
5.5.2
Ductile Fracture Mechanisms
Ductile fracture in metals is predominantly caused by void nucleation and microvoid coalescence.
Nucleation of voids inevitably occurs when stress is applied to materials which include second-
phase particles or inclusions. As the voids expand coalescence initiates, further increasing the size
of now combined voids [51] as shown in Figure 5.13.
Figure 5.13: Microvoid coalescence due to inclusions
46
==PAGE:79==
5. Fundamental Theory of Fracture Mechanics
Ductile crack growth also develops due to the growth and coalescence of voids in front of the
crack tip. As the crack blunts, the stress escalation and tri-axiality are adequate for void growth
until the distance between the crack front and enlarged void becomes small enough for coalescence
[20] as shown in Figure 5.13.
Figure 5.14: Ductile crack growth due to inclusion-induced voids
5.5.3
Brittle Fracture Mechanisms
Brittle fracture, opposed to ductile fracture, happens rapidly due to sudden unstable crack propaga-
tion. Cleavage failure is the predominant brittle fracture mechanism which occurs as transgranular
cracks along crystallographic planes, as illustrated in Figure 5.15. Cleavage may occur without any
prior macro-crack growth but can also be preceded by signiﬁcant plastic deformation and ductile
crack growth [26].
Figure 5.15: Brittle fracture by transgranular cleavage
Cleavage failure is initiated by stress peaks, which exceed the cohesive bond strength of the steel.
Given the cohesive strength of ferritic steels is in the order of E/π, there must be a discontinuity
ahead of the crack. Inclusions or carbides can crack due to the elevated plastic strain, forming
microcracks. The cleavage initiates in a single grain and rapidly propagates causing cleavage failure
through several grains [20] [52].
47
==PAGE:80==
==PAGE:81==
6
Literature and Norms for
Fracture Mechanics
BS7910-2019: Guide to methods for assessing the acceptability of ﬂaws in metallic structures [48]
speciﬁes procedures for assessing the capacity of a ﬂawed structural component. This is often
referred to as either an Engineering Critical Assessment (ECA), or a Fitness For Service assessment
(FFS). In this section, the theory and procedure behind an ECA analysis is elaborated upon.
6.1
The Failure Assessment Diagram
The failure assessment diagram (FAD) is a widely used methodology for elastic-plastic fracture me-
chanical analysis of structural components. By applying the FAD method, a non-linear problem
that is elastic-plastic fracture, is solved in terms of two parameters, the toughness ratio and load
ratio, which both vary linearly with the applied load. The FAD includes a wide range of material
behaviours, from complete brittle fracture under linear elastic conditions, to ductile behaviour in
the plastic regime, and everything in between. Additionally, the FAD approach is appropriate for
welded components, because residual stresses can be taken into account.
Originally, the FAD was derived from the strip yield model. Due to limitations of the strip
yield model such as not accounting for strain hardening, more accurate FADs were derived from
J-integral solutions. The most widely used are simpliﬁed versions of the FAD, where no rigorous
J-integral solution is required, but strain hardening is still accounted for [20].
6.1.1
Principle of the FAD
For a through thickness centre crack in an inﬁnite plate, the linear-elastic stress intensity factor is
determined by:
KI = σ√πa
(6.1)
Applying the strip yield model to prescribe an eﬀective stress intensity factor, corrected for crack
tip plasticity, the following expression is obtained:
Keff = σy
√πa
 8
π2 ln


1/2
(6.2)
The failure assessment curve (FAC) can be derived from the strip yield model by introduction
of the K-ratio, which is the ratio between the plasticity corrected SIF Keff and the LEFM SIF
49
==PAGE:82==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
KI, as seen in Expression (6.3), expressed as a function of the relative applied stress Sr = σ/σy.
f(Sr) =
KI
Keff
= σ
σy
 8
π2 ln

sec
 πσ
2σy
1/2
(6.3)
The introduction of the K-ratio expresses the driving force in a dimensionless form, eliminating
the square root term containing the crack dimension a. The same approach can be performed,
using Irwin’s crack tip plasticity correction, as presented in Section 5.3. The plasticity corrected
stress intensity factors can be seen compared to the linear elastic stress intensity factor in Figure
6.1a. It is clear from the plot that increasing stress magnitudes result in larger deviations from
LEFM. The right plot shows the derived FACs, f(Sr), from the two plasticity correction models.
(a) Plasticity corrected SIFs
(b) corresponding failure assessment curves
Figure 6.1: Plasticity correction and inﬂuence on failure assessment curves
The two criteria which limit the load-carrying capacity of a ﬂawed structure consist of the
following [53]: The linear elastic stress intensity factor must not be greater than the fracture
toughness.
The applied load must not be greater than the plastic collapse load of the ﬂawed
structure. These two criteria can be expressed as:
Kr ≤1
∧
Sr ≤1
(6.4)
These two criteria represent the two distinct failure mechanisms described in the previous.
However, interaction between the two mechanisms is to be expected. Therefore, the inequality of
Expression (6.4) is replaced with following:
Kr ≤f(Sr)
(6.5)
In short, a brittle material will fail when the K ratio reaches Kr = 1.0. Likewise, failure by
plastic collapse will occur when the stress ratio reaches Sr = 1.0. For intermediate cases, plastic
collapse and fracture interact, and both Kr and Sr are less than 1.0 at failure.
The main principle of the FAD is that all assessment points inside the FAC are safe, while all
50
==PAGE:83==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
points that fall outside the FAC are unsafe and pose a risk of failure. To assess the signiﬁcance of
a particular ﬂaw in a given structure, the toughness ratio and stress ratio should be assessed for
the ﬂawed structure. The toughness ratio and stress ratio are determined by:
Kr =
KI
Kmat
and
Sr = σ
σy
(6.6)
The shape of the FAD curve is essentially a function of plasticity eﬀects. Just like the plasticity
corrected stress intensity factors, FADs can be derived based on elastic-plastic fracture mechanics.
A J-solution provides a more accurate description of the elastic-plastic behaviour, and includes
strain-hardening, which is not included in the plasticity correction models. The applied J can be
converted into a stress intensity factor, K, through the following relationship:
KJ =
r
JE
1 −ν2
(6.7)
Substituting KJ for Keff in Expression (6.3), yields a new deﬁnition for the K-ratio, and thus the
FAC:
f(Sr) = KI
KJ
(6.8)
Figure 6.2a depicts the J-solution plotted against LEFM, similarly to Figure 6.1a. Non-linear
behaviour is captured by the J-solution with increasing applied stress, resulting in a divergence
from the LEFM. The resulting FAC shows a similar tendency: linear elastic conditions prevail with
small stresses and fK(σ) = 1. The shape of the FAC diﬀers from the FAC of Figure 6.1b since the
J-solution captures more plasticity eﬀects compared to the simpler plasticity correction models.
(a) SIF deviation from LEFM at increasing stress
(b) Corresponding failure assessment curve
Figure 6.2: Schematic plot of crack driving force, and corresponding FAD
Figure 6.2b visualises the importance of the capturing of the plasticity eﬀects in the FACs.
The dotted line represents the "FAC" based solely on LEFM. There is no stress dependency on
the failure assessment line and thus no interaction between brittle and ductile failure is captured.
Therefore the fracture criterion is governed by the fracture toughness, regardless of the stress mag-
nitude. In other words, this approach deems any ﬂaw as non-critical, given the toughness ratio is
less than 1, and the stress is less than the plastic collapse limit stress.
51
==PAGE:84==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
The the solid line, is based on EPFM, and thus takes the interaction between brittle fracture
and plastic collapse into account ensuring the K-ratio is a function of the applied stress. As seen
in the diagram, an example assessment point is included. The LEFM FAC predicts the ﬂaw as
safe implying that there is no risk of failure whereas the EPFM FAC predicts risk of failure.
Further generalising the FAD, the horizontal axis is normalised, by introducing the load ratio
Lr:
Lr = σref
σy
(6.9)
Where σref is the reference stress. Applying an external stress σ, the reference stress σref is the
resulting stress acting at the ﬂawed cross-section, as visualised in Figure 6.3. The reference stress
was originally introduced by Ainsworth, where he expressed it as [54]:
σref =
 P
P0

σ0
(6.10)
Where P is the applied load. The reference load, P0, usually corresponds to the load at which
the net cross-section yields. Likewise, σ0 acts as a reference, and usually is chosen to be the yield
stress.
Figure 6.3: Schematic visualisation of reference stress deﬁnition
The ﬁnal step of deﬁning the FAC is introduce the cut-oﬀvalue Lr,max, on the horizontal axis.
This cut-oﬀrepresent a limit load, i.e. plastic collapse criterion. For most steels, capacity past
the yield stress will be observed, and the ultimate limit stress thus is a value larger than the yield
stress. Therefore, the cut-oﬀvalue at which plastic collapse occurs is not necessarily at Lr,max = 1,
but can be a value greater than 1.
A typical failure assessment diagram can be seen sketched in Figure 6.4.

Figure 6.4: Sketch of typical failure assessment diagram
The FAD is a representation of the limiting driving force. In order to apply the FAD to assess
if a ﬂaw is critical or not, incorporation of the fracture toughness into the analysis is necessary.
This is simply done by plotting an assessment point into the FAD, where the x- and y-coordinates
of the point is determined by:
(Lr, Kr) =
σref
σy
,
KI
Kmat

(6.11)
Where KI is the linear-elastically determined SIF for the ﬂaw in question, and Kmat is the fracture
toughness of the material at the given conditions. The FAD can be applied to assess a single point,
or a sequence of points. The latter is applied when assessing fatigue, where a crack growth analysis
is performed while points subsequently are plotted in the FAD to assess at what point in time the
crack becomes critical.
(a) Crack growth curve
(b) Failure assessment diagram
Figure 6.5: Principle of crack growth analysis and concurrent failure assessment by FAD
The nature of the failure mechanism is a function of where the point falls horizontally. At
low toughness and applied stress (small Lr and large Kr), the failure occurs in the linear-elastic
range and the mechanism is brittle. Opposite, at large Lr and small Kr, the failure is out of the
53
==PAGE:86==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
linear-elastic range and the failure mechanism is ductile. For cases that fall in between the two
extremes, fracture occurs as an interaction between the two phenomena.
6.2
Defect Acceptance Criteria for Welded Connections
Post production, the welds are checked by non-destructive testing (NDT) to ensure that no ﬂaws
are outside the prescribed acceptance limits. This section will cover the current norm requirements
for maximum tolerable ﬂaw sizes as well as the limiting sizes that can be detected by current NDT
methods.
6.2.1
Norm Requirements for Welds
The defect acceptance limits for oﬀshore steel fabrication are governed by the applicable norms
and standards. DNVGL-RP-C203 [33] makes reference to DNVGL-OS-C401 [34] with regards to
acceptance criteria for various ﬂaw/defect types. C401 states that structural welds must comply
with criteria presented in ISO 5817 [55].
DNVGL-ST-0126 introduces a categorisation of structural members, namely "Primary", "Sec-
ondary" or "Special". The categorisation system can be correlated to the quality level system
of EN ISO 5817: Welds which belong to the category "Special" must conform with quality level
B and categories "Primary" and "Secondary" with quality level C. DNVGL-OS-C101 [36] gives
guidance to assigning structural categories, shown in Appendix A.5: Table A.3.
DNVGL-ST-0126 [35] states uni-axially loaded sections of monopile structures are categorised
as "Primary" but given the welds are fatigue critical details, they are re-categorised as "Special".
Therefore all welds must comply with the requirements presented for quality level B in accordance
to EN ISO 5817.
6.2.2
Flaw Detection by NDT
There are several methods and requirements for assessing ﬂaws in workshop welds. The "Primary"
circumferential welds belong to inspection category I, which is the most strict category, due to be-
ing prone to fatigue. Category I requires 100% of the weld to be examined by visual Inspection
(VI), MPI (magnetic particle inspection) and UT (ultrasonic testing).
The FKM guideline provides an overview of detectable ﬂaw sizes for various NDT methods
under standard fabrication conditions [30]. Table 6.1 shows a selection of NDT methods relevant
to this thesis.
54

VT and MPI can only detect surface ﬂaws and give an indication as to the planar extent of
the ﬂaw, i.e. the crack opening and length. NDT methods such as UT must be applied To asses
the depth of the ﬂaw, to get a representative ﬂaw size. Common for all inspection methods, the
probability of detection decreases with a decrease in ﬂaw size. This means that during inspection
of the welds, an expectancy of ﬂaws within the size range of the prescribed limits will with some
probability not be detected. DNVGL-RP-C210 prescribes formulae for determination of PoD curves
for various inspection methods [56]. The PoD as function of ﬂaw depth can be seen in Figure 6.6
for MT, UT and VI.
Figure 6.6: PoD curves from DNVGL-RP-C210 [56]
Most ﬂaws are for quality level C limited to a max depth of 0.5mm. From the curves of Figure
6.6, MT and UT methods detect 0.5mm deep ﬂaws with a 58% and 53% PoD, respectively. The
probability of detecting a 0.5mm deep ﬂaw by VI is signiﬁcantly lower, at 2.4%.

cracks and lack of fusion are not permitted no matter the size.

6.2.4
Initial Flaw Dimensions
The ECA procedure can be used for various purposes. Generally it is performed to assess either
of the following:
• Assess the maximum allowable ﬂaw size for a structure with a set target service life.
• Assess the remaining service life of a structure containing a ﬂaw of known dimensions, typi-
cally detected during inspection by NDT.
• Perform a probabilistic assessment in the case of the suspicion of ﬂaws in non-inspectable
areas.
• Verify structural components with regards to brittle fracture assessment, for geometries that
fall out of validity ranges of other norm procedures.
As the aim of this thesis is to apply the ECA procedure to optimise the fatigue assessment pro-
cedure for oﬀshore structures, the application of the ECA falls outside of the typical use. Hereby,
no direct speciﬁcations regarding choice of initial ﬂaw dimensions are to be found in BS7910, for
such assessments.
As a result of this, a basis on which the initial ﬂaws are determined should be established.
To ensure that the ﬂaw dimensions are chosen to be representative for what can generally be
expected in an oﬀshore structure, it is decided to apply the fabrication acceptance criteria for
oﬀshore structures. Various ﬂaw and defect types are allowed within strict sizing limits. Based on
this, combined with considerations regarding the probability of occurrence, as well as probability
of detection, will make basis for the initial ﬂaw dimensions of this study.
6.3
Fracture Mechanical Structural Assessment
Within BS7910 [48], procedures are given for assessing the acceptability of ﬂaws in a structure in
relation to their eﬀect on fatigue strength. For planar ﬂaws, fracture mechanical principles are
used to describe the behaviour of the ﬂaw. The procedure for fatigue assessment of a planar ﬂaw
will be introduced in the following sections.
56
==PAGE:89==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
6.3.1
Stress Intensity Factor, KI
For the sake of simplicity, and based on the nature of the loading in an monopile structure, mode I
loading is the most prominent, and it can therefore be assumed to be the only loading mode acting
on the ﬂaw.
BS7910 includes no SIF solutions for circumferential semi-elliptical surface cracks on the exter-
nal surface of a cylinder. Reference is made to a SIF solution for a surface ﬂaw in a ﬂat plate. An
extensive Literature study reveals that there is very little to be found regarding circumferential
surface cracks in pipes applicable for the dimensions of monopiles. Radju & Newman have de-
veloped stress intensity solutions for semi-elliptical surface cracks in pipes and rods [57]. Though
these stress intensity solutions were developed for R/t between 1 and 10. The monopiles of the
oﬀshore wind farm industry are of a much larger R/t ratios, and thus the developed SIF solutions
are not representative.
Furthermore the solutions are valid only for a/t ranging between 0.2 and 0.8, which means
that the solution is inadequate for the scope of this thesis. In a report developed by Chapuilot for
the french nuclear industry [58], SIF solutions for external circumferential semi-elliptical surface
cracks in pipes are presented, which are valid for geometries in the magnitude of the oﬀshore
wind monopile substructures. The solutions allows for determination of stress intensity factors,
for a polynomial load distribution through the wall thickness as presented in Section 5.1.2. The
Chapuilot solutions for stresses varying with a polynomial form up to third order. The SIF is
expressed by:
KI =

i0 · σ0 + i1 · σ1
a

+ i2 · σ2
a
t
2
+ i3 · σ3
a



(6.12)
The solution will be addressed in detail in Section 7.2.3, including an elaboration of the coeﬃ-
cients in the expression.
Solutions developed by Raju & Newman [59], are presented in BS7910 for semi-elliptical surface
crack in ﬂat plates. For monopiles of diameters similar to the structural basis (7000mm), the
curvature present at a surface ﬂaw, assuming that all ﬂaw dimensions are lesser than the wall
thickness of the monopile, could potentially be negligible. Since the ﬂat plate SIF solution is much
simpler to apply, it is of interest to determine the deviation of the ﬂat plate from pipe solutions,
in the dimension regime that the oﬀshore monopile lie within. To investigate this, a parameter
study is performed, comparing the ﬂat plate and pipe solutions, for a variety of ﬂaw and plate/pipe
geometries.
6.3.1.1
Stress Intensity Comparison
A comparison of the ﬂat plate and the Chapuilot pipe SIF solutions has been performed for four
diﬀerent crack dimension aspect ratios. The geometry function Y is calculated for both solutions,
and is plotted over the normalised thickness in Figure 6.7. This is done for relative crack depths
57
==PAGE:90==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
up to 80%, which is the max depths at which the Chapuilot pipe solution is applicable.
Figure 6.7: Comparison of ﬂat plate and pipe SIF solution, and relative deviation for various a/c
ratios
The thickness applied is 78mm, and an outer diameter of 7000mm, corresponding to the struc-
tural input. Observed from the plot is that both SIF solutions follow each other closely over the
entire thickness, and for all aspect ratios, although there evidently are deviations between the
two. This small comparison reveals that while applying the ﬂat plate solution for this R/t-ratio
is a relatively close approximation, there still is a diﬀerence between the two solutions. The pipe
solution will still be a better representation of the geometry in question.

6.3.2
Stress Intensity Magniﬁcation Factor, Mk
As mentioned in Section 2.1, the introduction of welds, also introduces stress concentrations. When
determining the inﬂuence of the SCF on the crack, it is not enough to simply multiply the stress
by an SCF, as the actual magniﬁcation factor will be a function of the crack depth.
Mk for
FPBWs is inﬂuenced by several parameters, namely: the plate thickness, weld width, weld toe
angle, reinforcement height and weld toe radius as well as the crack depth and width [61] [62]. Mk
is usually determined from FEA, by ﬁrst modelling a geometry with a crack with no attachment to
give rise to a stress concentration and subsequently the cracked geometry including attachments
and/or welds.
59
==PAGE:92==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
(a) SIF with weld
(b) SIF with no weld
Figure 6.9: Deﬁnition of Kweld and Kno weld
The principle can be seen in Figure 6.9, and Mk is then determined by the following expression:
Mk =
Kweld
Kno weld
(6.13)
BS7910 presents a simpliﬁed expression for determination of Mk to ensure applicability over a
broad range of diﬀerent weld geometries, deduced from 2D FEA. The simpliﬁed expression is used
to determine the stress concentration present at a T-joint, a cruciform joint and a full penetra-
tion butt weld. With the simpliﬁcation, conservatism follows, and as a result does not represent
the stress concentrations at butt welds with great precision. An additional parametric solution is
presented, formed on the basis of 3D FEA, but assumes a weld toe angle θ = 45◦, therefore still
introducing unnecessary over-conservatism.
A similar expression has been proposed by DNV [56], which takes the plate thickness, crack
depth, crack aspect ratio, weld toe angle, weld footprint and any grinding into account. Utilising
the supplied geometry and requirements presented in the norms, the curves presented in Figure 6.10
are compiled, for both a weld toe angle of 15◦and 30◦. A weld toe angle of 15◦is allowed for butt
welds classiﬁed under the D-curve of DNVGL-RP-C203, even though the fabrication acceptance
criteria for the welds allows for an angle of 30◦.

6.3.3.2
Crack Growth Increments
When performing crack growth analyses without dedicated fracture mechanics software and for
cases where the crack growth cannot be described by a closed-form solution, the analysis must be
performed using an incremental approach, where load cycle incrementation is one way of perform-
ing the analysis. When applying spectrum loading, load cycle incrementation is advantageous,
as it allows for the splitting of diﬀerent load magnitudes and number of cycles with ease. When
applying load cycle incrementation it is of utmost importance that the increments are kept small
enough such that the assumption of a constant ∆K is representative [30]. If the increment size is
too large, the is a large risk of gross under estimation of the crack growth, as visualised in Figure
62
==PAGE:95==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
6.13
Figure 6.13: Visualisation of the error introduced by increment sizing
An alternative incrementation method is crack extension incrementation, where ∆a is gradually
increased, but will not be covered in this thesis.
6.3.4
Fracture Toughness, Kmat
Based on the temperature dependency of the material toughness exhibited by virtually all steels,
a way of determining the fracture toughness Kc or Kmat as function of operating temperature is
required. Master curves can be used for this exact purpose and are essentially expressions describing
fracture toughness at a given temperature, with the probability of failure as an additional variable.
Figure 6.14 illustrates the concept of the fracture toughness master curve. The curve is based
on experimental data, and is calibrated to the speciﬁc steel in question by the T0 value, which is
deﬁned as the temperature at which the material reaches a fracture toughness of 100 MPa√m.
Figure 6.14: Fracture toughness master curve example
63
==PAGE:96==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
Within BS 7910 [48], a master curve formulation of the fracture toughness is presented, as
follows:
Kmat = 20 +

1/4 

1/4
(6.15)
Here T is the temperature at which Kmat is to be determined in ◦C, also denoted the service
temperature. T0 is estimated from the Expressions in (6.16) . TK is the temperature term that
describes the scatter in the correlations described by Equations in (6.16). Generally TK = +25◦C
is used, which is based on a standard deviation of 15 ◦C and 90% conﬁdence interval. A lower
value for TK may be used if supported by experimental data. t is the thickness of the material in
mm and Pf is the probability of Kmat being less than estimated. Pf = 0.05 is recommended by
BS7910 unless experimental data is available as evidence to support the use of a higher probability.
For ferritic steels, a correlation between the 27 J and the 40 J Charpy transition temperatures
(T27J and T40J) and the 100 MPa√m fracture toughness transition temperature, T0, in a 25mm
thick specimen is described by the following expressions.
T0 = T27J −18◦C
T0 = T40J −24◦C
(6.16)
If Charpy-V values have been obtained for other impact energies than 27J or 40J, a SINTAP
report authored by A.C Bannister presents a method of interpolation between values [63]. The
interpolation is based on the impact toughness testing temperature and the corresponding absorbed
energy. The absorbed energy is read oﬀthe graph leading to a value intersecting the abscissa, which
can be used to determine the interpolated T27J from Equation (6.17). An example is presented
in Figure 6.15 and continued below in Equation (6.17) for a specimen yielding 51J at -60◦. The

6.3.5
Static and Cyclic Stresses
In order to assess a ﬂaw with regards to fatigue, the ﬂuctuating loading and corresponding stress
conditions surrounding the ﬂaw must be well deﬁned. The stresses should be assessed based on
the unﬂawed structure and only stress concentrations due to gross structural discontinuities are
included in the calculation of the applied stress. Any local structural discontinuities, such as weld
geometry and the crack shape, are taken into account in the calculation of KI.
The actual stress distribution over the section can be used for the assessment, for example
by use of the inﬂuence coeﬃcient method or weight functions (Section 5.1.2). Alternatively, a
linearisation may be performed in order to simplify the assessment. Two ways of linearisation are
permitted; across the ﬂaw length and across the entire section. In Figure 6.16, examples of both
linearisation methods are presented. As apparent in the ﬁgure, linearisation across the section
tends to overestimate the stresses to a greater extent than distributions linearised over the ﬂaw.
The advantage of using a distribution linearised over the section width, is that no re-calculations are
required, when the crack dimensions change. All linearised distributions are acceptable provided
that the stress is greater than or equal to the magnitude of the actual distribution over the ﬂaw
surface.
Figure 6.16: Examples of linearisation of stress distributions both across the ﬂaw and across the
section
65
==PAGE:98==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
For the case of ﬂuctuating loads, in the form of a stress spectrum, it should be converted into
a discrete number of stress ranges. This can be done by any method of choice, such as rain ﬂow
counting or reservoir methods. With the stress data converted into a histogram of stress ranges
and number of occurrences, each block in the histogram must be assumed to experience the largest
stress range within the block, as seen in Figure 6.17.
Figure 6.17: Example of stress range spectrum discretised into blocks
A Schematic representation of the stress distribution across a section can be seen in Figure 6.18.
In the ﬁgure, a) and c) represent the primary membrane and bending stresses. b) and d) show how
discontinuities and stress concentrations, which are not accounted for in KI lead to magniﬁcations
of the stress compared to a) and c). Stress concentrations from misalignments must be included,
as per e). In addition to this, the secondary stresses, such as welding residual stresses, must also
be included in the fracture mechanical assessment, represented by f). All these stress contributions
and concentrations all are superimposed to a combined stress proﬁle of an arbitrary form, as seen
in g).
Figure 6.18: Visualisation of stress superposition on a arbitrary cross section
6.3.6
Load Application Sequence
BS7910 states that fatigue loads in the form of a VAL spectrum should be applied in the most
damaging order, if the exact application order is unknown. Since the most damaging order is not
66
==PAGE:99==
6. Literature and Norms for Fracture Mechanics
always apparent, several combinations should be assessed.
FKM introduces the block splitting approach, where a load spectrum is split into multiple blocks
and applied sequentially [30]. This approach generally allows for crack growth estimate more true
to nature, but reduces conservatism. The approach will be elaborated in Section 7.2.4.1.3
A study investigating the inﬂuence of load application order, performed by Maddox and Zhang
[64] shows the application order is critical to the fatigue lifetime.
The study concluded that
applying stresses in the most detrimental order also resulted in failure faster than less critical load
application orders. Furthermore, the Palmgren-miner summation was found to be non-conservative
for the most critical load application order and led to failure at n
N ≈0.8.
6.3.7
Residual Stresses
With regards to crack opening actions on circumferentially welded monopile cans, transverse resid-
ual stresses acting perpendicular to the ﬂaw are of interest. There is a level of uncertainty when
assessing residual stresses in welded joints, therefore conservative assumptions regarding the mag-
nitude and distribution through the thickness exist.
For structures in the as-welded condition, the simplest assumption to be made is constant stress
proﬁle with a magnitude of the "appropriate" yield stress, σy′. For ﬂaws parallel to the weld, the
"appropriate" yield stress is the lesser of the yield strength of the parent and weld material, at
room temperature.
Some global relief of residual stresses may be assumed as a result of the primary loading applied,
to the as-welded structure. The (constant) membrane residual stress may then be assumed to be:
σT

Where, σref is the reference stress as a result of the applied load and σf ′ is the appropriate
ﬂow strength. This can be assumed as the average of the yield strength σy and the tensile strength
σu.
Alternatively, residual stress distributions of higher order polynomial shapes can be assumed,
for a more realistic representation, and a reduction of over-conservatism. For circumferential butt
welds, three distributions are available, and the distribution that should be used for a given weld
is dependent on the applied weld heat input E, and the plate thickness t. These distributions
can be ﬁtted to lower order polynomials and applied by use of the inﬂuence coeﬃcient method
or alternatively the weight function method. The distributions are presented in Table 6.4. The
distributions can be seen visualised, normalised over member thickness in Figure 6.19.

It is noted that even though residual stresses are self-equilibrating stresses, the area under
the three stress distributions presented in Figure 6.19 do not equal zero. Integrating the three
distributions would in all three cases yield a positive (tensile) total stress.
Hereby, the stress
distributions are conservative, including more tensile, i.e crack opening stresses, than a true self-
equilibrating stress state would. It is also seen that all three distributions assume a stress magnitude
of yield stress at the top surface (z/t = 0), insinuating that the proﬁles have been calibrated/shifted
to comply with the assumption of residual stress of yield stress magnitude. This might also explain
the stresses being out of equilibrium.

stresses should be accounted for. In accordance to BS7910 the toughness ratio can be determined
by either one of two ways, as follows:

The reference stress σref represents the stress in the ﬂawed cross section, induced by the

stress must only be determined based on actions that contribute to plastic collapse. Most secondary
stresses, here-under residual stresses do not contribute to plastic collapse, and should therefore not
be included in the calculation of the reference stress.

7
Monopile Assessment by Fracture
Mechanics
An Engineering Critical Assessment will be performed in section section, based on the input de-
ﬁned in Section 4.2 and the previously presented fracture mechanical theory and norm procedures.
Firstly the input will be covered, followed by the selection of relevant fracture mechanical param-
eters, material toughness, load application methods and initial ﬂaw geometry. Finally the results
will be summarised, analysed and compared to the S-N procedures from Section 4.3. The authors
of this thesis have developed a fracture mechanical assessment tool, which allows for assessment
of cracks relevant to the investigations performed in this thesis. The program has been named
ECAT, short for "Engineering Critical Assessment Tool".
7.1
Developed ECA Tool - ECAT
ECAT is a program that performs a combined fracture mechanical crack growth and fracture anal-
ysis. ECAT consists of a set of MATLAB scripts, employing an algorithm resembling explicit
integration scheme. Crack growth rates and ﬂaw dimensions at a particular point in time are as-
sumed to be constant during a time increment, in order to evaluate the next point in time. The tool
has been developed such that the user with great customisability can model a variety of problems,
including diﬀerent ﬂaw shapes, various correction factors and consideration of complex residual
stress distributions, amongst others. Detailed information regarding the procedure of ECAT and
all the inherent features can be found in Appendix C: Developed Engineering Critical Assessment
Tool.
A key feature of the script is the option to specify a incremental tolerance. This is included in
order to limit the error introduced at each explicit integration step. The user can specify the vari-
able ε to limit the accepted crack dimension growth, relative to the previous step. The phenomenon
introduced in Figure 6.13 is by this tolerance countered automatically in the analysis. Specifying
a value of e.g. ε = 0.005 indicates that any increment, the crack cannot grow more than 0.5% of
the previous crack dimensions. If the crack does grow more than the limit, the increment size is
decreased and the step is reverted. A smaller tolerance results in a larger amount of increments.
Thus the tolerance impacts the computational time, and should therefore not be assigned too small.
Commercial fracture mechanical software which applies a similar procedure is also available.
The Welding Institute (TWI) oﬀers an extensive program, CrackWISE® [66],which is widely used
to perform crack growth and fracture analyses. To gain conﬁdence in the procedure and output of
ECAT, a benchmark against CrackWISE® is performed. A simple problem subject to constant
75
==PAGE:108==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
amplitude fatigue loading is analysed by the two software, with the exact same input, and the
resulting crack growth curves, FADs and critical crack dimensions are compared.
7.1.1

The details of the benchmark test, such as case and ﬂaw geometry, loads etc. can be found in
Appendix I.1: Semi-Elliptical Surface Crack in Flat Plate - CrackWise. The case is a semi-elliptical
surface crack in a ﬂat plate. A sketch of the geometry can be seen in Figure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Sketch of plate and crack geometry and dimensions

Figure 7.2: ECA results for ECAT vs CrackWISE®

ECAT produces identical results to the commercial software. With this benchmark, the proce-

available in CrackWISE®.

considered conservative. The relevant characteristic material properties for 78 mm S355 NL steel
is σy = 325 MPa and σu = 470 MPa.

7.2.4
Loads
7.2.4.1
Fatigue Loads
To investigate the inﬂuence of loading sequence, three diﬀerent load application methods will be
performed, namely the DEL approach, the most critical sequence and the block application method.
Common for all load application methods is that they are based on a variable amplitude fatigue
data. Figure 7.5 visualises the Markov matrix containing the fatigue loads at the elevation of the
chosen critical detail.
Figure 7.5: Visualisation of Markov matrix of fatigue loads
7.2.4.1.1
Simpliﬁed DEL approach
The simpliﬁed DEL approach consist of transforming the various stress ranges and corresponding
cycles into a single Damage Equivalent Load, as presented in Section 2.3.2. The markov matrix
79
==PAGE:112==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
of Figure 7.5 yields a DEL stress range of ∆σeq = 31.6MPa, for a reference number of cycles
Nref = 1 · 107 over 31 years.
7.2.4.1.2
BS7910 "Most critical sequence"
As per BS7910 it is recommended in the case of spectrum loading that the loads are applied in the
most damaging order. Since a threshold ∆Kth for crack growth is included in the crack growth
model, applying the loads in a descending order will allow more stress ranges to induce stress
intensity ranges above the threshold, causing elevated crack growth rates.
(a) Non scaled fatigue loads (k=1)
(b) Scaled fatigue loads (k=0.9)
Figure 7.6: Schematic visualisation of scaling principle in "most critical sequence" load
application method
In order to achieve a representative crack growth curve, the number of cycles must be scaled
such that the crack becomes critical exactly after all cycles have been applied.
If this is not
done, the fatigue loads occur a number of times corresponding to the original 31 years.
This
could potentially result in the initial high stress cycles inducing a crack growth so large that not
all subsequent cycles can be applied before the crack dimensions become critical. If this is the
case, it indicates that the analysis predicts a lifetime less than 31 years, and the crack will have
experienced too many cycles with large stress ranges, and not enough cycles with low stress ranges.
The principle is visualised in Figure 7.6, where the scaling factor k is introduced. As seen in Figure
7.6a, not scaling the number of cycles (k=1), results in the crack reaching critical dimensions, prior
to all cycles being applied. In this case, 31 years worth of high stress have been applied, but the
calculated life time is less than 31 years, which means that too many large stress ranges have been
applied. In Figure 7.6b, a scaling factor of k=0.9 has been applied, indicating a reduction of 10%
for all cycles. As seen in the ﬁgure, the crack becomes critical at the very last load cycle. This
indicates that the fatigue loads have been scaled exactly to match the life time of the structure.
Thus the life time of the structure is in this example determined to be 31 · 0.9 ≈28 years.
7.2.4.1.3
Annual Block Application Method
Statistically it is extremely unlikely that the load history in reality will occur in the "most critical
sequence". Generally speaking, natural phenomena such as storms and large currents occur on a
80
==PAGE:113==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
seasonal basis. For an example, historical data of the Paciﬁc ocean show that the vast majority
of typhoons have happened between May and November [67]. It can therefore be argued that
the Markov matrix representing the entire design life can with reasonable precision be split up
in annual Markov matrices, assuming the structure experiences the same number of cycles for all
stress ranges every year. The principle is seen visualised in Figure 7.7, where a load histogram is
split up into blocks. The approach is presented in the FKM and is less conservative than the most
critical sequence approach, but is more representative of the actual load sequence on the structure.
The loads of the Markov matrix in Figure 7.5 are split into 31 blocks, since the full Matrix contains
fatigue loads for the entire 31 year service life.
Figure 7.7: Visualisation of block splitting load histogram
7.2.4.2
Extreme Load
The extreme load will be applied as a single "static" load after each crack growth increment, to
assess the risk of failure. For the sake of the fatigue assessment comparison, the "static" load
applied in calculation of the FAD assessment points needs to correspond to the failure criterion
inherent in the S-N curves. For this reasoning, the extreme load is set to be the tensile part of the
max stress range found within the fatigue load Markov matrix. As seen in Figure 7.5, the largest
stress range is ∆σ = 212.8MPa, at a mean stress of ¯σ = 1.7MPa, yielding a max tensile stress of
σmax = 108.1MPa. This tensile stress will be applied as the extreme load for the FADs of the ECA
analyses performed in the following.
7.2.4.3
ALS load
In the case of the brittle fracture assessment, the ALS load should be applied to be in line with the
current brittle fracture procedures. As previously mentioned, ALS loads have not been supplied
in the input report, and have thus been assumed to be equal to the ULS loads with the partial
safety factors deducted. The max tensile ALS stress then is σALS = 105.6MPa.
7.2.4.4
Secondary Stresses
The secondary stresses, from welding residual stresses, will be accounted for during the crack
growth phase, by increasing the load ratio, R, to R = 0.5 as described in Section 5.4. Additionally,
the secondary stresses will be included when determining the toughness ratio, Kr, as per Section
81
==PAGE:114==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
6.3.8.2. Seeing as secondary stresses do not contribute to plastic collapse, they have no inﬂuence
on the the load ratio Lr.
An example of a typical welding procedure speciﬁcation (WPS) for a double-sided FPBW of two
80mm thick plates has been provided by a undisclosed steel manufacturer [68]. DNVGL-OS-C401
allows WPQRs for plates to be used for cylinders, if the external diameter of the cylinder is greater
than 500 mm [34], subsequently being applicable to the monopile in question. The maximum heat
input speciﬁed in the WPS is E = 3.7kJ/mm which corresponds to a thickness-normalised heat-
input of E/t = 46.3J/mm2. Conservatively assuming the same heat input for the slightly thinner
78mm wall thickness, the normalised heat-input equals E/t = 47.4J/mm2. Based on Table 6.4,
the through-thickness residual stress distribution for "Low" heat input may be assumed, and is
expressed by:

The residual stress distribution is expressed by a 4th-order polynomial. The stress intensity
solution applied for the ECA allows for polynomial stress distributions no higher than 3rd-order.
Since the crack is not expected grow through the entire thickness before the risk of failure arises,
a 3rd-order polynomial ﬁt is performed to ﬁt as much of the true distribution as possible. As seen
in Figure 7.8a, the ﬁtted 3rd-order polynomial ﬁts the true distribution well up until z/t ≈0.8.
This is in line with the choice of stress intensity factor, which is valid for crack depths up to 80%
of the thickness. The ﬁtted through-thickness residual stress distribution is expressed as follows:

distribution. It is observed that the ﬁtted distribution stays within ∼±5% relative deviation over
the entire applicability range. Exceeding z/t = 0.8, the deviation rapidly accelerates, and the
ﬁtted expression is not a valid representation of the true distribution beyond this limit.

7.2.5
Initial Flaw Size
As presented in Section 6.2, cracks and lack of fusion are not tolerable no matter the extent. Un-
dercutting is acceptable up to a depth of 0.5mm in accordance to EN ISO 5817 [69]. Undercuts are
generally not regarded as planar ﬂaws unless the depth exceeds 1 mm [48], therefore assuming the
initial crack depth to be 0.5mm based on the weld ﬂaw requirements is conservative and should be
representative as a worst case initial condition. ISO 5817 refers to EN-ISO 11666 regarding The
maximum tolerable ﬂaw lengths. For linear indications inspected by magnetic particle inspection
(MPI/MT) the maximum length is given as l ≤3 mm [70].
Flaw shape speciﬁcation based on inspection data is prescribed in BS7910. If a ﬂaw has been
detected by inspection and the depth and length is measured, it should be assumed that the ﬂaw
shape is described by an ellipse, where the two half-axes correspond to the measured dimensions.
Therefore, the initial ﬂaw size will be assumed as a semi-elliptical surface ﬂaw, with a depth
a =0.5 mm and half crack width c = 3 mm. The assumed crack depth furthermore coincides with
the upper-bound value for assessment in accordance to DNVGL-RP-C203, if no exact data is
available [31].
Figure 7.9: Flaw dimension characterisation in accordance to BS7910
As previously established, the most critical location of a crack in the monopile is adjoining the
circumferential welds. This is the case that will be analysed in the ECAs. A sketch of the location
of the crack in a cut-out of a MP can be seen in Figure 7.10.
83
==PAGE:116==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
Figure 7.10: External surface ﬂaw at the weld toe
7.2.6
Crack Growth Model
In accordance to BS7910, a two-stage crack growth Paris-Erdogan crack growth law is adopted for
the fatigue analysis. BS7910 states that welded structures should use crack growth parameters for
stress ratio R > 0.5, regardless of the actual stress ratio, to take the eﬀect of residual stresses into
account.
Based on the information presented in Section 4.2, the critical weld should be assessed as-
suming crack growth parameters for submerged members, with cathodic protection. A contour
plot depicting the averaged galvanic cathode protection (GACP) for the external surface has been
supplied by COWI A/S, and can be seen in Appendix E.4: Figure E.2. From the contour plot it
is apparent that the averaged protection potential in the embedded region of the MP is approxi-
mately −850 mV. Therefore, the two-stage crack growth model for steel in a marine environment
with cathodic protection potential at -850 mV (Ag/AgCl) is adopted for the ECA. The crack
growth parameters are summarised in Table 7.2. As discussed in Section 6.3.3, the mean crack
growth parameters yield a PoF of 50% and the mean +2SD a PoF of 2.3%. The crack growth
parameters have been modiﬁed to represent an addition of 3 standard deviations, such that the
probability of failure obtained in the ECA is in the same order of magnitude as the S-N curves
with a DFF=3. The standard deviation has been determined based on the mean and +2SD crack
growth parameters, enabling an additional SD to be added to the parameters.

7.3
Results of Engineering Critical Assessment
7.3.1
Transport and Installation Loading
Prior to applying the in service loads, the damage accumulated during installation should be as-
sessed and added to the initial ﬂaw. The fabrication tolerances are inspected and veriﬁed prior to
the structure leaving the fabrication yard, and there is thus no guarantee that the ﬂaws have not
grown in size in the time between leaving the factory, up until the structure is installed and ready
to be commissioned.
For the structural basis, no stress history is supplied for the transportation phase. Therefore
it will be omitted from the analysis, and only installation lads are assessed. Likewise comparisons
to the S-N method will be made without the transport contribution.
As presented in Section 4.2, the MPs are installed by pile driving, exerting 2000 hammer blows
to the top of the MP, ramming it down into the seabed. The stress range introduced by the blows
is ∆σ = 123MPa, assumed to be constant over all 2000 blows.
Due to the relative short installation period no corrosion eﬀect is assumed to occur. Therefore
the pile driving damage calculated by the conventional S-N approach allows for the use of a S-
N curve for a structure in air, instead of a corrosive environment. To ensure consistency in the
comparison between the S-N approach and the FM approach, a similar assumption is made for the
fracture mechanical analysis of the installation damage. Crack growth parameters for structures
86
==PAGE:119==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
in air presented in Table 7.2 are used for the analysis.
(a) Crack depth, a
(b) Crack width, l
Figure 7.13: Crack growth curves for installation loads
The resulting crack growth curve can be seen in Figure 7.13. The increase in crack dimensions
is small during the installation procedure, growing from a = 0.5mm to a = 0.5469mm in depth.
Likewise the crack width grows from l = 3mm to l = 3.0721mm. The new ﬂaw dimensions will be
applied as initial ﬂaw dimensions in the subsequent analyses.
7.3.2
Damage Equivalent Fatigue Loading
A DEL moment range of ∆Meq =91.17MNm converted to an equivalent stress range of ∆σeq =
31.61MPa has been applied. The reference number of cycles is Nref = 1 · 107, over the 31 years
target life time. The resulting crack propagation curve can be seen in Figure 7.14, accompanied by
the corresponding FAD. The ﬁnal crack dimensions are ac = 58.6mm and lc = 167.2mm, becoming
critical after N = 8.47 · 106 cycles.
(a) Crack growth curve
(b) Critical FAD point: B
Figure 7.14: ECA results for constant amplitude DEL fatigue loads
87
==PAGE:120==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
7.3.3
Most Critical Sequence Fatigue Loading
The fatigue loads are applied in a single block, in descending order. To achieve a representative
crack growth curve, the number of cycles must be scaled such that the crack becomes critical
exactly after all cycles have been applied. The scaling factor has been obtained by an iterative
process, to ensure that the crack dimensions becomes critical after all cycles are applied. The ﬁnal
scaling factor applied in the results of Figure 7.15 is k =0.805, which corresponds to loads for
31 · 0.805 = 24.95 years. The critical crack dimensions are ac = 56.0mm and lc = 178.8mm
(a) Crack growth curve
(b) Critical FAD point: B
Figure 7.15: ECA results for critical order fatigue loads
7.3.4
Block Applied Fatigue Loading
To apply the fatigue loads by annual block application, all bins of the matrix, containing number
of cycles Ni,i, have been divided by 31. The result of this is a Markov matrix containing the mean
annual number of cycles. The annual Markov matrix can be applied X times, corresponding to
applying loads for X years.
(a) Annual loading sequence: A1 (high to low)
(b) Annual loading sequence: A2 (low to high)
Figure 7.16: Visualisation of the two annual load application sequences
The blocks are applied in two diﬀerent sequences, which can be seen in Figure 7.16. The blocks
88
==PAGE:121==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
of sequence A1 are applied in descending order, whereas sequence A2 is in ascending order. The
results from the two ECA analyses can be seen in the two plots of Figure 7.17a and 7.17b.
(a) Crack growth curve (A1)
(b) Crack growth curve (A2)
(c) Crack growth curve (A3)
(d) Critical FAD point: B
Figure 7.17: ECA results for block applied fatigue loads
In addition to the two former block sequences, a third sequence is evaluated. The Markov
matrix is split into monthly blocks and the stresses are applied in descending order. The sequence
is named A3, and 12 blocks corresponds to one year. The resulting CGC can be seen in Figure
7.17c, accompanied by the FAD in Figure 7.17d, which is similar for all three cases. All ﬁnal crack
dimensions, and number of cycles can be seen in the summary of Table 7.3.

sarily translate to a linear crack growth assumption. Therefore, only the end fatigue life, i.e. the
end points should be related to the crack growth curves of the ECA analyses. Additionally, S-N
results are included applying both the actual plate thickness (t = 77.5mm), as well as the eﬀective
thickness determined by Expression (3.3), (teff = 60.2mm). It is apparent from the ﬁgure that
applying the eﬀective thickness increases the fatigue life by ∼5 years, compared using the actual
plate thickness. The ECA results also yield fatigue life much closer to the S-N curve applying the
actual thickness.

varying up to 5 years, the ﬁnal crack dimensions are very similar for all cases. Since the extreme

7.3.6
Brittle Fracture
As discussed in Section 3.2, the brittle fracture is assessed as an ALS stress state. The extreme
stress applied in the ECAs is σmax = 108.1MPa, which is greater than the ALS stress of σALS =
105.6MPa. Since the two stresses are very close in magnitude, only diﬀering 2.5MPa, and the stress
applied in the FAD assessments is greater than the ALS stress, it is not necessary to run additional
analyses. The FAD results of the previously discussed ECAs therefore deemed representative for
the brittle fracture assessment.
7.4
S-N Relation by Fracture Mechanics
Another way of comparing the S-N fatigue damage procedure with the fracture mechanical model,
is to evaluate the S-N relation predicted by fracture mechanics. Constant amplitude stress range
analyses ranging from 10MPa to 250MPa are performed in ECAT, monitoring the number of cycles
until failure. The data points can be seen in a regular plot in Figure 7.20a, and a log-log plot in
Figure 7.20b.
(a) Regular x- and y-axis
(b) Logarithmic x- and y-axis
Figure 7.20: Fracture mechanical S-N analysis result plots
As discussed in Section 3.1.1, the S-N curves applied in fatigue design are two-staged and
follow a power law which is log-linear. The data points in Figure 7.20b indicate that the FM
91
==PAGE:124==
7. Monopile Assessment by Fracture Mechanics
results similarly show log-linear tendencies, also with a shift in the slope around N = 105 cycles.
An analytical S-N curve in the form of Expression (3.1) can be obtained based on the data points
and represented by a power law:
y = bxa
⇒
∆σ = bN a
(7.11)
In a log-log plot a power law appears as a linear function, where the power coeﬃcient, a
corresponds to the slope, and the constant, b corresponds to the intercept with the vertical axis.
Thus, the S-N relationship can be expressed in the form of a linear expression:
log(y) = a · log(x) + log(b)
⇒
log(∆σ) = log(b) + log(N) · a
(7.12)
Isolation of the log-term containing the number of cycles N, yields following expression:
log(N) = log(∆σ) −log(b)
a
(7.13)
Introducing the following two deﬁnitions, a S-N relation can be expressed of the same format as
the S-N expression from DNVGL-RP-C203 (Expression (3.1)).
m = −1
a
,
log(¯a) = log(b) · m
(7.14)
log(N) = log(¯a) −m · log(∆σ)
(7.15)
This procedure has been applied on the data points in Figure 7.20 twice, based on the bi-linear
tendency. The resulting S-N curve parameters are summarised in Table 7.4.

Supplementary Numerical
Fracture Mechanical Analysis
The section will cover the FEA assessment of fracture mechanical parameters, based on a section of
a monopile. The SIF, K1 is evaluated for several intermediate crack sizes taken from the analytical
results, to compare with the estimates of the analytical expressions. Furthermore, a circumfer-
ential weld is introduced into the model, to evaluate the stress magniﬁcation factor Mk by FEA
and again comparing to analytical expressions. Benchmark tests have been performed prior to the
actual analyses, to ensure correct modelling of simple and well known geometries before moving
on to more complex models. All analyses are performed in Abaqus.
Abaqus allows for the extraction of SIFs by applying a domain integral method, which assesses
a tubular volume around the crack front [72]. The domain integral method acts as a 3D method
for the J-integral described in Section 5.2 [73]. When extracting SIFs for LEFM by use of the
J-integral, the following expression is used to determine K from J:
J = 1 −ν2

front will be embedded and a contour area which will be used for the contour domain integral to
determine the SIFs. Figure 8.2a shows a ﬂat plate, with partitions made for the crack front and
contour areas. Figure 8.2b shows a zoom of the plate, where the dotted red area has been removed,
revealing the semi-elliptical partition.

on the crack depth, where l is the longest side of an element. An overview of the mesh and close-ups

Figure 8.4: Monopile mesh and zoom of contour area and crack front mesh
The analyses are based on results from the 3rd contour integral as per the Abaqus user manual
[75]. Studies performed by Qian et al. [76] and Courtin et al. [73] show domain independence
from the 3rd integral. To verify these studies a test was performed on crack nr. 3 to investigate
the percentile growth from increasing the number of integrals. Table 8.3 shows how the geometry
function Y at the deepest crack point increases for an increasing number of contour integrals,
showing the increase in accuracy is 0.1% when moving from the 3rd to the 4th contour integral,
indicating domain independence. Therefore the 3rd contour integral is assumed to be suﬃciently
accurate. Furthermore, domain independence signals a satisfactory mesh [72].
