9
Evaluation of Results
This section will cover an evaluation of the ﬁndings as a whole. Firstly the applied input for the
ECA will be discussed, followed by the results from both the ECA and the conventional methods
and ﬁnally the FEA optimisation of fracture mechanical parameters. Subsequently a conclusion
based on the thesis as a whole will be presented, followed by a brief mention of possible further
works.
9.1
Discussion
9.1.1
Initial Crack Size
The initial ﬂaw size applied in the ECAs is based on the norm acceptance criteria of allowing a
3 mm long undercut with a depth of 0.5 mm, while conservatively assuming the undercut acts a
sharp defect. Assuming that the ﬂaw already is planar disregards the entire initiation phase, which
reduces the fatigue life time. This is further backed up by DNVGL-RP-C203, regarding 0.5 mm as
an upper-bound conservative assumption [33].
A brief analysis has been performed in ECAT to assess the design-life sensitivity to initial crack
size. An initial aspect ratio of a
c = 0.33 is applied to all cracks. The initial depths analysed are:
0.1 mm, 0.2 mm, 0.3 mm, 0.4 mm and 0.5 mm. A DEL of ∆σ = 31.6 MPa has been applied as in
Section 7.3.2. Figure 9.1a shows the sensitivity to the initial crack size, demonstrating the initial
size of the ﬂaw has a signiﬁcant impact on the fatigue life of the structure. This is in line with
investigations performed by Mehmanparast et al. [77], which showed a considerable sensitivity to
the initial crack size assumed for a jacket structure.
(a) Crack growth curves
(b) %-increase in fatigue life
Figure 9.1: Inﬂuence of initial crack size on fatigue life
Figure 9.1b schematically presents the impact a reduction of the initial ﬂaw depth has on the
103
==PAGE:136==
9. Evaluation of Results
fatigue life. The plot presents the %-increase in fatigue life (relative to fatigue life for a=0.5mm),
as function of initial ﬂaw depth. As seen in the ﬁgure, this can with great accuracy beﬁtted to a
power-law for the speciﬁc case. The resulting relation is shown in Expression 9.1.
%-increase = (59.04 · a−0.722) −100
,
a ≤0.5mm
(9.1)
The conservatism introduced by disregarding the initiation phase, can be reduced by assuming
a slightly smaller crack. Given the fatigue initiation phase is assumed to make up 10-30% of the
entire fatigue life [28], a reduction from a = 0.5mm to a =0.4 mm could based on Expression (9.1)
potentially be applied, in order to account for the initial sharpening of the crack.
9.1.1.1
Fracture Toughness
The fracture toughness for the ECA is based on master curve approach, which correlates the
Charpy-V impact energy to a fracture toughness. The methodology is generally seen as very con-
servative, given the high scatter of Charpy-V tests. Despite being very conservative, the method is
accessible for most structures post fabrication, as not all structures have fracture mechanics-based
toughness tests performed. If the tests have not been performed prior to installation, obtaining
data can be diﬃcult as test specimens cannot be taken from the structure in service, therefore
the use of existing Charpy-V impact toughness data is convenient [78].
Also when Generally
designing structures, the material properties are based in characteristic values. Speciﬁc material
properties are usually not available in design phase of a project and fracture mechanical test data is
not available. Therefore the master curve approach serves as a versatile option, for almost all cases.
The fracture toughness estimated for the service temperature of T = 0◦C is Kmat = 106.9 MPa√m,
according to the master curve. DNVGL-OS-C401 [34] states that where fracture mechanics testing
is required, the critical CTOD must not be less than δc=0.15mm, tested at the service temperature
or less. A relation between CTOD and fracture toughness is given in the following expression [49]:
KCT OD =
p
1.5δcσyE′
(9.2)
For the 78mm thick plate, the resulting fracture toughness based on the CTOD criteria becomes
Kmat = 177 MPa√m. This is a signiﬁcantly greater toughness compared to the estimate from the
master curves. The applied fracture toughness is thus on the conservative side, and application of
fracture mechanical testing could result in greater toughness values to be used in ECA analyses.
9.1.2
Stress Intensity Factor Solution
As previously established, literature regarding fracture mechanics on hollow cylinders in the scale
of monopiles is scarce, and so are stress intensity solutions which are applicable to MP dimensions.
The primary SIF solution applied in the ECAs is Chapuilot’s solutions for hollow cylinders.
Referencing BS7910, there is no SIF solution representing the monopile geometry and ﬂaw type.
In this absence, BS7910 makes reference to the ﬂat plate solution (see Appendix B.1: Semi-Elliptical
104
==PAGE:137==
9. Evaluation of Results
Crack in Flat Plate), allowing for the assumption that the ﬂat plate solution is an adequate approx-
imation for cracks in cylinders. Considering the ratio between the outer radius and the initial crack
size for the case in question, the curvature over the ﬂaw is extremely small, and thus it is possible
that the ﬂat plate solution represents case with satisfactory accuracy. Figure 6.7 demonstrates that
the Chapuilot solution agrees closely with the R&N ﬂat plate curves, with the ﬂat plate yielding
more conservative results for shallower relative crack depths. A slight downside of the Chapuilot
solution is the limitation of the maximum allowable relative crack depth of a
t = 0.8, although the
critical crack size proved to be reached reached prior to a
t = 0.8 this for the monopile.
Another downside of the Chapuilot solution is the fact that it is based on inﬂuence coeﬃcients,
and thus only accepts stress proﬁles described by polynomial, further limited to maximum 3rd-
order polynomials. Alternatively, stress intensity solutions for surface ﬂaws in ﬂat plates based
on weight functions could have been used. Application of a ﬂat plate solution, based on weight
functions instead would allow for application of the exact residual stress distribution. The inﬂuence
of this will be discussed further in Section 9.1.6.
Despite this, the Chapuilot solution was developed to better represent the stress intensity factor
of a circumferential ﬂaw in a cylinder, instead of accepting ﬂat plate solutions to be representative.
The inﬂuence of the cylindrical shape and hereby the deviation from ﬂat plates dissipates as the ra-
tio between the radii and the crack width of the cylinder increases. But as the crack width increases,
the deviation will likewise increase. Additionally, application of ﬂat plate solutions would require
an assumption of constant membrane stress through the thickness, to represent global bending of
a cylinder wall. The application of weight functions would eliminate this limitation, but would
greatly increase the computational time of the analyses. Therefore the Chapuilot solution proves to
best represent the MP geometry while being most eﬃcient with regards to computational demand.
The parametric MP solution proposed by Mehmanparast et al. only provides a solution for the
deepest crack point and a limiting validity range for 0.2 ≤a
t ≤0.8 which limits its applicability
of modelling crack growth. The MP solution could possibly be used for the determination of a
maximum allowable ﬂaw size with regards to brittle fracture.
9.1.3
Crack Growth Law
ECAT utilises a simple Paris-Erdogan crack growth law. The crack growth law is only strictly ap-
plicable to Region II in the crack growth diagram. More accurate crack growth laws can substitute
the Paris-Erdogan law and incorporate modelling of regions I and III, but require more material
speciﬁc parameters which are not prescribed in BS7910.
Furthermore when assessing history dependent variable amplitude loading, load interaction
eﬀects can aﬀect the crack growth. A generalised Wheeler approximation can be applied to modify
the Paris-Erdogan law which can account for retardation by assessing plasticity ahead of the crack
tip due to overloads as well as account for crack arrest and acceleration [30]. A study performed by
Meggiolaro & de Castro [79] showed that the modiﬁed Wheeler model yielded the most accurate
105
==PAGE:138==
9. Evaluation of Results
results compared to other load interaction models.
9.1.4
Crack Growth Parameters
The ECA employs 2-stage crack growth laws from BS7910 for R ≥0.5, for both in-air for the
pile driving phase and protected in seawater for the in-service phase. The crack growth laws are
modiﬁed from mean+2SD to mean+3SD to accommodate a lower PoF of ∼0.1% which better cor-
responds to a DFF=3, generally applied to submerges MP sections. Furthermore, a lower-bound
stress intensity threshold value of ∆Kth = 2 MPa√m is employed for the crack growth analysis.
An investigation performed by Mehmanparast [80] investigated the crack growth in a double-
sided FPBW on two 90 mm S355 plates both in air and seawater. The crack growth curves were
obtained for CT specimens transverse to the weld seam in the HAZ. The investigation showed that
the simpliﬁed crack growth law was a better ﬁt to the obtained test data than the mean +2SD
curves for material in the HAZ for both in-air and in seawater, as shown in Figure 9.2. Furthermore,
the tests indicated a threshold value of ∆Kth closer to 13 MPa for 11 out of 12 specimens for both
in-air and seawater, with a single specimen indicating lower values. Despite the better ﬁt, there is
insuﬃcient conservatism when applying the simpliﬁed curve, as several points were exactly on the
curve for the data for in-air.
(a) Fatigue crack growth for HAZ (Air)
(b) Fatigue crack growth for HAZ (Seawater)
Figure 9.2: Fatigue crack growth data on HAZ for air and seawater compared to BS7910 crack
growth laws (Mehmanparast, 2017 [81])
9.1.5
Limit Load Solution, Lr
The ECA uses the limit load solution proposed by Dillström, which encompasses structural collapse
of a cylinder with an external semi-elliptical ﬂaw. The plastic collapse of a monopile structure
subjected to bending loading, cannot be approximated accurately by use of a limit load solution
for a plate subjected to axial loading. When assessing the collapse of an entire cylinder, there is
substantially more reserve capacity for redistribution as opposed to a ﬂat plate subjected to axial
106
==PAGE:139==
9. Evaluation of Results
loading. Figure 9.3 shows the impact on the FAD for a ﬂat plate under axial loading versus a
cylinder under bending loading. The ﬂat plate solution is based on the solution from BS7910.
(a) FAD (Flat plate Lr solution)
(b) FAD (Pipe Lr solution)
Figure 9.3: FADs for DEL for ﬂat plate and pipe Lr solutions
9.1.6
Residual Stresses
The distribution of welding residual stresses through the thickness of a member is a function of
many variables, and it is problematic to establish analytical expressions and literature regarding
the subject is therefore very scarce. The boundary conditions, welding procedure, heat input and
material properties of both base and weld material are a few of the key parameters that inﬂuence
the magnitude and shape of the residual stresses. Mathew et. al [82] presents measurements of
welding residual stress distributions in welded stainless steel cylinders. The steel has σ ∼300MPa,
and the thickness of the diﬀerent specimens are 25-35mm, with radii between 2-5 times the mem-
ber thickness. The shape and size of the specimens are not representative for monopiles, but the
mechanical material properties and the boundary conditions of cylinder specimens resemble that
of monopiles.
Figure 9.4 presents a contour plot of the through thickness welding residual stress distribution
in a t = 25mm thick specimen with radius R = 112.5mm. Apparent is that the stress proﬁle
is constant over the circumference of the specimen, with slight deviations.
The inside of the
wall experiences compressive stresses while the outside experience tensile stresses. The heat input
applied in the welding procedure is E = 2.5kJmm−1, which translates to a normalised heat input of
E/t = 100Jmm−2. Referring to the residual stress distributions of BS7910, this welding procedure
would allow for application of the stress distribution for "Medium" heat input for austenitic steels,
which has the same distribution as "Low" for ferritic steels (see Table 6.4). Referencing Figure
6.19, the shapes of the measured residual stress distributions in Figure 9.4, mostly resemble the
distribution for "low" heat input, which was applied in the ECAs. The maximum stresses near the
outer surface are tensile, nearing the yield stress. Nearing the inside surface the stresses approach
the yield stress in compression. The distribution of BS7910 similarly prescribes a reduction in
107
==PAGE:140==
9. Evaluation of Results
residual stresses near the inner surface, not below ∼50% of the yield stress in tension.
This
indicates that the distribution of BS7910 is conservative, though the results of Figure 9.4 are for
thicknesses much lesser than the monopile case.
Figure 9.4: Two-dimensional axial stress map, and through thickness stress proﬁles [82]
Qiang et al. [83] has performed combined experimental and detailed numerical analysis of a
double-sided multi-pass weld in a 30mm thick steel plate. The steel grade is σy = 345 MPa - similar
to the steel grade of the monopile. A comparison of the measured and the numerically determined
residual stresses are presented in Figure 9.5. The transverse stresses are measured and simulated
to be below ∼0.75σy of the yield stress, throughout the entire thickness for both compression and
tension. Since these results are from welded plates, the boundary conditions diﬀer from those of
a circumferential weld. Therefore both the shape and magnitude of the stress distribution might
not be representative for the welds in monopiles.
Figure 9.5: Numerically and experimentally determined residual stresses in 30mm welded steel
plate [83]
No literature or studies regarding residual stresses through the thickness of plates or cylinders
with dimensions similar to the monopile have been found.
The previously introduced ﬁndings
are used as a reference, to which the BS7910 distribution is compared. Although the two studies
might not be representative of monopiles, the results can to some extent be used to indicate what
can be expected of both magnitudes and shapes of the residual stress distribution in welds. The
108
==PAGE:141==
9. Evaluation of Results
tendencies and shapes of the BS7910 distributions show what based on the ﬁndings of Mathew et.
al. and Qiang et. al can be considered conservative.
9.1.6.1
Inﬂuence of Residual Stresses
Residual stresses are included in the fracture mechanical analysis in two ways; assuming that
R ≥0.5 for crack growth, and inclusion of the stress intensity factor for secondary stresses, Ks
I.
The residual stresses thus inﬂuence both the crack growth rate and the eﬀective toughness ratio
Kr. This means that the residual stresses have detrimental impact on the fatigue resistance, hence
why welded joints are extremely prone to fatigue. If a more accurate residual stress distribution can
be obtained, the inﬂuence on the results of an ECA could potentially be signiﬁcant. Furthermore,
the assumption of R ≥0.5, could be replaced by an actual evaluation of the R-ratio of the stresses
acting on the crack surface. For the case of varying stress-proﬁles, the stress ratio could potentially
be substituted by a K-ratio as follows:
R = Kmin
Kmax
(9.3)
Introduction of this consideration could potentially allow some stress intensity ranges to obtain
values of R < 0.5, allowing for use of more favourable crack growth parameters, and potentially
extending the life time predicted by the ECA.
9.1.6.2
Approximation of Residual Stress Distribution
Recalling Section 7.2.4, the residual stress distribution is approximated by a 3rd-order polynomial,
to allow for application of the Chapuilot SIF solution.
In order to establish if the inaccuracy
introduced by this approximation is tolerable, a comparison of the stress intensities calculated
applying weight functions is performed. An SIF solution for a semi-elliptical surface crack in a ﬂat
plate, developed by Shen et. al. [84] which applies weight functions allowing for the application of
any stress distribution, is used as a reference.
(a) K for ﬂat plate solution and pipe solution
(b) Relative deviation
Figure 9.6: Comparison of K for ﬂat plate and pipe applying weight function
109
==PAGE:142==
9. Evaluation of Results
As presented in Figure 9.6a the stress intensity factor is plotted for the ﬂat plate solution
applying both the true residual stress distribution as per BS7910 (4th-order) and the approximate
distribution (3rd-order).
Note that the comparison is performed only within the applicability
limits established in Section 2.1.1, which is up to 80% of the plate thickness. For the ﬂat plate
case, the K determined for the approximate distribution follows the K determined with true stress
distribution closely. Figure 9.6b presents the relative deviation between the two, which for a
t > 0.1
yield satisfactory results. Given that the residual stress distribution only contributes to the failure
assessment and not the crack growth, the approximation is deemed applicable, given the majority
of cracks cause failure when a
t > 0.1.
9.1.7
Load Application Method
As presented in Section 7.2.4 three diﬀerent main load application methods have been applied for
the ECA: the DEL, block loading and most critical sequence. Although BS7910 prescribes that the
most critical loading sequence should be applied to structures and components where the loading
sequence is unknown, this is seldom a representative method. Therefore both the simpliﬁed DEL
and block applications methods have been applied. As expected, the DEL method yields conser-
vative results, as the most critical Wöhler exponent is applied to determine the DEL. The block
application method was applied on an annual basis, with the stresses applied in both ascending
and descending order. The results showed a load-application sequence dependence, with the de-
scending order yielding the most conservative results, as the largest stresses had propagated the
crack further, causing a greater SIF for the following lesser loads. Finally the block application
method was applied for loads in descending order on a monthly basis, which resulted in a crack
growth curve in between the ascending and descending annual methods.
The ECA analyses proved that the load application method and sequence impacts the fatigue
life.
Application of larger stresses ﬁrst rapidly increases the size of the crack.
A larger crack
directly translates to a larger value of K. This means that if all stress ranges above the threshold
stress intensity range are applied ﬁrst, the subsequent smaller stress ranges, that previously were
below the threshold, now are above, and further contribute to the crack growth. This results in
a shorter fatigue life, which could be observed from the crack growth curve comparison of Figure
7.18. The analysis that yielded the shortest fatigue life applied all loads in a descending order.
Correspondingly, the analysis yielding the largest fatigue life was the one split into annual blocks,
where the block-wise application order was ascending. Low stresses below the threshold are applied
ﬁrst, meaning no crack growth takes place before stress ranges exceed the threshold value.
Given that storms occur on a seasonal basis, a block application method more accurately
models the actual crack growth. With that being said, there is no certainty that an equal number
of high and low stresses occur each year. The nature of the loads on marine structures is highly
stochastic, and deviations from the mean expectancy is bound to occur. Three major storms could
occur one year, with no storms the next year. Therefore there is a probability that an increased
number of large stresses early in the design life would result in a decrease the fatigue life, based
110
==PAGE:143==
9. Evaluation of Results
on the loading sequence dependency.
Therefore, the annual block application method may be
unconservative without appropriate statistical considerations.
9.1.8
Current Procedure for Fatigue Assessment
The current procedure for fatigue assessment is speciﬁed in DNVGL-RP-C203 which builds upon
the S-N approach. The current procedure takes the structural detail type, thickness, size and local
stress concentrations into account as well as the environment. The S-N curves which form the
basis of DNVGL-RP-C203 are based on S-N data from 100’s of test specimens. Since they are
empirically developed, they inherently account for fatigue initiation time. Despite this, they are
based on relatively small specimens, which do not allow for the redistribution of stresses but also
exhibit higher fracture toughnesses [31]. Furthermore, the S-N curves do not account for variables
such as temperatures during loading, nor the inﬂuence of ULS loads when nearing failure.
The key assumption of S-N based fatigue, is the linear damage accumulation. The methodol-
ogy is centred on the fact that the damage from variable amplitude loading can be superimposed
linearly. This implies that the application sequence is not a factor - the order in which the loads
occur have zero inﬂuence on the end fatigue life.
The ECAs of this thesis all yielded diﬀerent fatigue life times. The results varied as much as
∼5 years between each other, corresponding to variation up to 16% relative to the design service
life. These results strongly indicate that the loading sequence is a factor, of which the fatigue
life is dependent, indicating that linear damage summation can be unconservative, coinciding with
investigations performed by [64].
Furthermore, BS7910 prescribing the most critical sequence
should be applied also indicates that the loading sequence inﬂuences the fatigue life.
9.1.9
S-N curve by Fracture Mechanics
An S-N curve has been developed based on fracture mechanics, disregarding the T&I phase. The
developed S-N curve has been used to calculate the fatigue damage, by the conventional linear
damage accumulation approach.
Deq has been determined by applying the DEL and Dtot by applying Palmgren-Miner’s damage
accumulation method on the full Markov matrix. The resulting damages can be seen summarised
in Table 9.1. Recalling Table 7.4, the FM S-N curve parameters are very close to the design S-N
curve when applying the true plate thickness, t. This is conﬁrmed in Table 9.1, where both Deq
and Dtot from the FM S-N curve are -0.4% and -3.8% less than the design curve, respectively.
Table 9.1: Fracture mechanical and design S-N curve damage summary

Compared to the design S-N curve applying the eﬀective thickness teff however, the FM S-N

linearity) and the close match to the design S-N curves indicates that the fracture mechanical model
established, and the chosen initial ﬂaw dimensions, represent the actual behaviour to a reasonable
degree. As discussed for the initial ﬂaw size, a reduction of the initial ﬂaw from 0.5 to 0.4 mm to
account for fatigue initiation, yields an increase of 20% on the fatigue life, which would bring the
FM S-N curve even closer to the design curve for teff.
9.1.10
Current Procedure for Brittle Fracture
The procedure of veriﬁcation against brittle fracture is independent of the actual fatigue damage
accumulated during the service life of the structure for both DNVGL-ST-0126 and EN 1993-1-10.
9.1.10.1
EN 1993-1-10
As it was demonstrated in the background investigation of EN 1993-1-10 [19], the tabulated lim-
iting thicknesses are based on a simple fracture mechanical assessment. The crack size at which
the risk of brittle fracture is assessed corresponds to a accumulated damage of D=0.25 [85]. Thus
roughly translated, the risk of brittle fracture is only assessed at a single point in time, which
corresponds to one quarter of the design life.
Based on the ECAs of Section 7.3.5, the FADs showed that the risk of brittle fracture, is
dependent on the crack dimensions which derivatively means that the capacity against fracture is
a function of time. The extreme loads occur at random points in time, and the structure is just
as likely to be subjected to fracture inducing loads in the ﬁrst year of service as in the last year.
This contradicts the assumption of EN 1993-1-10 procedure. A structure veriﬁed against brittle
fracture by this approach, would therefore be subject to increased risk of brittle fracture once the
structure has passed 25% of the design life, assuming that the thickness of the structure is equal
to the limiting thickness. Considering the general shape of the crack growth curve over time, the
crack size at D=25% is insigniﬁcant. This is visualised in Figure 9.7.
Figure 9.7: Principle of relation between crack size and damage indicator
112
==PAGE:145==
9. Evaluation of Results
It should be noted that the crack size at D = 0.25 is determined by a simple fracture mechan-
ical crack growth analysis, which inherently assumes a sharp crack is present from the beginning,
ignoring the initiation phase. Since initiation is disregarded in the fracture mechanical model, a
level of conservatism is introduced. Assuming that the ﬁrst 25% of the structural life is initiation
of the crack, the crack growth analysis would represent the crack dimensions closer to D=0.5,
corresponding to the yellow area of Figure 9.7.
The crack growth parameters applied in the calculations for determining the limiting thicknesses
of EN1993-1-10 are presented in the literature [85]. In Figure 9.8a the crack growth law can be
seen compared to BS7910 crack growth laws including the additional +3SD parameters developed
for the ECAs. Note that the law implemented in EN 1993-1-10 is simpliﬁed single-stage law, as
compared to the two-stage laws of BS7910.
(a) Crack growth laws
(b) Crack growth curves
Figure 9.8: Comparison of the crack growth laws and curves of BS7910 (Air and -850mV), and
EN 1993-1-10
Crack growth analyses using the crack growth parameters have been performed on the MP
geometry with the damage equivalent stress in ECAT. All resulting crack growth curves can be
seen in Figure 9.8b. Interestingly, the crack growth parameters applied in EN1993-1-10 yield a
signiﬁcantly greater life time, compared to both +3SD and +2SD, not only for -850mV corrosion
protected steel, but also for steel in air. Only mean crack growth parameters of BS7910 yield fa-
tigue lives greater than the EN 1993-1-10 parameters. Based on this comparison, the crack growth
parameters of EN 1993-1-10 are deemed unconservative.
The initial ﬂaw size used to perform the crack growth analysis in EN 1993-1-10 are assumed to
be a function of the plate thickness. This is done to account for an increase of fabrication tolerances
and presence probability of larger defects. For the case of an member with a nominal thickness of
78 mm, the initial crack size is aini = 2.18mm. An analysis with this initial crack size has been
113
==PAGE:146==
9. Evaluation of Results
performed, and the resulting crack growth curve can be seen included in Figure 9.8b (black dotted
line). While this yields a signiﬁcant reduction in the life time, it still is considerably greater than
both the +2SD and +3SD crack growth curves, for both in air and corrosion protection. From
this it can be deducted that application of the recommended crack growth curves and parameters
of BS7910, would yield a reduction in the tabulated limiting thicknesses of EN 1993-1-10.
The case that makes basis for the underlying analysis of EN 1993-1-10 is a ﬁllet-welded trans-
verse stiﬀener plate. This geometry is of a considerably more critical fatigue class (56MPa) com-
pared to the transverse butt weld (90MPa), in accordance to EN 1993-1-9 [86]. Since the tabulated
limits are based on a detail with low fatigue resistance, they do not represent the actual capacity
of joints with better fatigue detail categories. This implies that the limits conservatively act as an
envelope.
The tabulated limits are based on an assumption of welding residual stresses of, constant over
the thickness with a magnitude of 100MPa. Based on the residual stress considerations in BS7910,
and the previously discussed measured and numerically determined residual stress distributions,
this assumption is by no means conservative. Residual stresses of magnitudes up to yield stress
are to be expected in welded joints.
9.1.10.2
DNVGL-ST-0216
No background literature has been found regarding the tabulated values of DNVGL-ST-0126.
DNVGL-OS-C101 [36] states that members which do not abide by the maximum permitted wall
thicknesses should assessed by a FFS analysis, based on fracture mechanical testing of representa-
tive geometries.
9.1.11
Supplementary fracture mechanical FEA
The FEA benchmark showed satisfying results, conﬁrming the mesh convergence and domain in-
dependence for further analyses. Additional checks for domain independence were performed for
the MP models, showing the 3rd was suﬃcient.
9.1.11.1
Geometry Functions
In general the FEA of the MP yielded lower values for the geometry function compared to all
applicable analytical expressions, for both bending and axial loading, at the deepest point as well
as at the crack ends. Figures 9.9a and 9.9b show the percentile deviation between the FEA and
analytical geometry functions for axial loading at the deepest point and the crack ends respectively.

Figure 9.9a shows both the Chapuilot and R&N solutions yield more conservative values for
all relative cracks depths at the deepest point, a, with a diverging trend for increasing crack sizes,
reaching approximately 3%. Figure 9.9b shows the FEA results predict larger values for the ge-
ometry function for the initial crack size, compared to the Chapuilot solutions at the crack ends,
but generally lower for subsequent relative crack depths. The trends for the Chapuilot and R&N
for the crack ends are similar, with the FEA results yielding between 2-3% lower values.

parametric MP solution only determines the geometry function for the deepest point.

115
==PAGE:148==
9. Evaluation of Results
to the Chapuilot solution at the crack ends as it did for axial loading.
Comparing Figures 9.9 and 9.10 it is clear that Ya shows a slight dependency on the loading
type, with a larger relative deviation for Chapuilot in bending loading as opposed to axial loading
where the results are almost identical.
If FEA was used to model the crack growth, subsequently applying FEA based KI along the
crack front, a reduction in the range of 5−10% could possibly be achieved for ∆K and subsequently
the crack growth rate, signiﬁcantly increasing the fatigue life.
9.1.11.2
Stress Magniﬁcation Factor
The stress magniﬁcation factor values obtained by FEA proved to estimate lower values compared
to the prescribed analytical expressions in DNVGL-RP-C210, as was seen in Figure 8.8.
The
percentile deviation between the FEA obtained results and the DNVGL analytical solution for Mk
is shown in Figure 9.11a. The FEA yields signiﬁcantly lower values for Mk, especially for smaller
relative crack depths. This can be due to the fact that the DNV method proposed in C210 is
an envelope solution designed for a broad range of weld geometries, therefore the results for some
geometries may be overly conservative.
(a) Percentile deviation of Mk FEA vs. DNV
(b) Percentile deviation of Mk Fitted vs. FEA
Figure 9.11: Percentile deviation for FEA, DNV and ﬁtted Mks
Figure 9.11b shows the percentile deviation between the proposed ﬁt for Mk and the FEA
results. The ﬁtted curves are conservative for all discrete relative crack depths with a maximum
overestimation of 3.5%.
The deviations between the envelope solutions and the DNV solutions are shown in Figure
9.12a. The proposed envelope solutions yield reductions for the majority of relative crack depths,
except for 0.08 < a
t < 0.23 and 0.66 < a
t ≤0.7. In these regions, the ﬁtted curve for the crack ends,
c, over-predicts by ∼1%. The reduction compared to DNV is most profound for shallower rel-
ative crack depths, where a reduction also has the greatest eﬀect, given the crack growth exponents.
116
==PAGE:149==
9. Evaluation of Results
The reduction of Mk, opens for the possibility of using FEA software to avoid excessive con-
servatism. The proposed geometry speciﬁc envelope solutions have been implemented in ECAT
to evaluate the impact of the stress magniﬁcation on the results. In Figure 9.12b, crack growth
curves for the DEL approach and annual block splitting approach for both the DNV expressions
and the proposed expressions have been plotted.
(a) Percentile deviation of Mk ﬁtted vs. DNVGL
(b) CGCs for Mk from DNVGL and ﬁtted Mk
Figure 9.12: Percentile deviation for ﬁtted Mk vs. DNVGL expressions and inﬂuence on fatigue
life
The comparison of the CGCs in Figure 9.12b show the reduced stress magniﬁcations greatly
aﬀect the crack growth rate, and as a result the lifetime of the structure. Application of the FEA
developed Mk achieved an increase in fatigue life of 159% and 207% for DEL and block application
methods respectively. The signiﬁcant increase in fatigue life can be contributed to the crack growth
exponent m, which for the shallow relative crack depths takes the value of 5.1 for the ﬁrst stage
of the 2-stage crack growth laws. A 10% reduction of Mk for the shallow relative crack depths
therefore leads to a ∼42% reduction of ∆K and thereby also the crack growth rate, explaining
the drastic increase of fatigue life.
117
==PAGE:150==
9. Evaluation of Results
9.2
Conclusion
This chapter encompasses overall conclusions in regard to the investigations carried out, their
results and appertaining discussions. Conclusions on the fracture mechanical model are presented,
followed by a conclusion on the comparison of the conventional design procedures and the fracture
mechanical procedure. Lastly, a conclusion on the possibilities of optimisation of oﬀshore steel
substructures is made.
9.2.1
The Fracture Mechanical Model
• A semi-elliptical surface ﬂaw, with a = 0.5 mm and l = 3 mm, conservatively assuming a
sharp undercut yielded conservative fatigue lives as expected. The fatigue life showed con-
siderable sensitivity to initial crack size, following a trend that with a power-law ﬁt describes
the relative % increase of fatigue life with a reduction in crack size extremely well. A 20%
reduction in crack size increased the fatigue life by 20%, thereby allowing for the considera-
tion of the initiation phase.
• The applied Chapuilot pipe solution was deemed the most representative for the monopile
structure. The ﬂat plate solution could be used for smaller crack sizes, as the curvature of
the MP is negligible but for increased cracks sizes, the deviation correspondingly increased.
Furthermore, the pipe solution allows for consistency considering the applied Lr solution.
The Mehmanparast solution yielded less conservative results but was ruled out due the strict
relative crack depth validity range.
• The Paris-Erdogan law is deemed suﬃciently accurate and conservative for the case in hand.
The law adopted in the analyses was a two-stage formulation. If additional speciﬁc material
parameters could be obtained, the Forman & Mettu crack growth law could be incorporated
to account for all three regions of crack growth. Alternatively incorporation of a Wheeler
approximation to account for load interaction when applying the block loading method, could
further improve the accuracy of the model.
• The crack growth parameters applied in the ECA are conservative, applying mean+3SD
curves for R ≥0.5.
Given the conservative assumption of welded components ensuring
R ≥0.5 through the entire thickness, some reduction in crack growth could be achieved if
the actual R-ratio was determined for each load application and the respective crack growth
curve for the corresponding R-ratio could be used. Furthermore, studies have shown the
mean+2SD and assumption of ∆Kth = 2 MPa to be overly conservative, with the simpliﬁed
crack growth laws showing better ﬁts.
• Establishing the fracture toughness based on the Master Curve approach is conservative,
but is a widely applicable method, as Charpy-V impact energy values are readily values.
118
==PAGE:151==
9. Evaluation of Results
Alternatively the minimum requirements from norms and standards can be applied, with
conﬁdence that it is a conservative estimate. Alternatively fracture mechanics testing can be
performed for critical location, which for the case in hand could have increased Kmat by 65%.
• When assessing the failure of a structure, an accurate representation of the geometry is nec-
essary when assessing the limit load for plastic collapse. If an inaccurate solution is applied
an incorrect indication of the failure mechanism and critical ﬂaw sizes can be obtained. The
limit load solution for ﬂaws in cylinders proved to yield much greater load capacity, as com-
pared to the ﬂat plate limit load solution, which reﬂects the larger cross-sectional area of a
cylinder as compared to a plate.
• The residual stress distributions from BS7910, which are applied in the ECA, have proved
to be conservative. While actual residual stress distributions are in equilibrium, the applied
distributions are in tension, i.e. crack opening through the entire thickness. Furthermore
the assumption of yield stress magnitude at the surface is conservative, with studies showing
stresses of lesser magnitudes are to be expected at the surface. The 3rd-order ﬁtted polyno-
mial for the residual stress showed a satisfactory ﬁt for areas of interest.
• The most critical sequence was deemed overly conservative and non-representative, therefore
the simpliﬁed DEL method was applied. The DEL method also yielded conservative results,
as the method is based on the largest Wöhler exponent for all stress ranges. Based on a
statistical consideration of storm histories, the annual block method was employed, which
resulted in the least conservative design life, but is deemed most representative of real-world
loading sequences.
• The numerical investigations performed have shown the analytical solutions predict larger
values for both the stress intensity factor, KI (up to 5%), and the stress magniﬁcation factor
Mk (Up to 15%). The Mehmanparast parametric function for a is closer to the FEA results,
over predicting by less than 2% for all analysed crack depths. By employing FEA to determine
geometry functions it is possible to reduce the crack growth rates and subsequently increase
the fatigue life of the structure. Case-speciﬁc ﬁtted expressions were determined, acting as
envelope solutions, deviating by a maximum of 3.22% with respect to the FEA results. The
proposed expressions reduce the maximum Mk by almost 14% increasing in fatigue life by
159% and 207% for DEL and annual block application respectively. The signiﬁcant reduction
in Mk is deemed unconservative and would not be recommended for use.
9.2.2
Conventional Design Procedures and Fracture Mechanics
The conventional S-N curve approach is based on the assumption of linear damage accumulation.
This translates to an assumption of no dependence of the load sequence.
The validity of this
119
==PAGE:152==
9. Evaluation of Results
assumption was investigated by a fracture mechanical fatigue analysis, where several fatigue load
application schemes were applied.
• The fracture mechanical model was employed to develop a S-N relation. The resulting S-N
curve proved to match the norm design curves exceptionally well. The determined fatigue
damage from the fracture mechanical S-N curve was ∼30% greater than the design S-N
curves, which could be attributed to the neglecting of the crack initiation phase in the fracture
mechanical model. This corresponded to studies, reporting that the crack initiation phase
constituting between 10%-30% of the fatigue life. The analyses resulted in a clear indication
of loading sequence dependency on the fatigue life. Between the diﬀerent loading sequences,
diﬀerences in fatigue life reached upwards of ∼5 years, corresponding to a deviation of ∼16%
relative to the design fatigue life. From the investigations it is concluded that there is a
dependency on the loading sequence, which is not accounted for by conventional S-N fatigue
design procedures.
• The current brittle fracture assessment procedure proved to be based on a number of as-
sumptions, some of which deemed conservative, and others unconservative. The overall level
of conservatism is therefore challenging to judge, but deemed the monopile case safe against
brittle fracture. The fracture mechanical analyses proved that brittle fracture was the domi-
nant failure mode towards the end of the fatigue life, and that the risk of brittle fracture was
present in all cases, prior to reaching the 31 year design fatigue life.
9.2.3
Conclusion on Optimisation Possibilities
The objective of this thesis was to investigate if fracture mechanics could be employed to optimise
the design of oﬀshore steel substructures. Out of all design considerations focus was made on
fatigue and brittle fracture - both of which related to crack propagation. The FAD approach was
adopted to evaluate the combination of brittle fracture and fatigue damage accumulation. This
approach proved to yield fatigue life times in the same order of magnitude as the S-N curve ap-
proach, although shorter, likely due to not including the fatigue initiation phase.
The established model employed considerable levels of safety, on all parameters, including:
the residual stress distribution, crack growth parameters, fracture toughness, failure assessment
diagram, stress intensity magniﬁcation factor, stress intensity solution and the initial ﬂaw dimen-
sion, all of which were chosen to be conservative. The mean+3SD crack growth curves result in a
PoF=0.1%, which roughly equals the same for a 20 year design life with a DFF=3 with the S-N
approach. It is therefore assumed that the S-N approach for a design life of 31 years, has a larger
PoF than the FM approach. Based on this, a level of over-conservatism may be present in the
fracture mechanical model, compared to the S-N approach.
Based on these considerations, the possibility of optimisation by employment of fracture me-
chanical theory as opposed to the conventional approaches can not be ruled out. Fracture mechanics
proved to represent both the fatigue and brittle fracture phenomena exceptionally well, and further
120
==PAGE:153==
9. Evaluation of Results
calibration of the approach to balance the level of conservatism, and increase the complexity of
the analyses could possibly yield improved fatigue life, and brittle fracture prediction.
9.3
Further Work
In addition to the investigations carried out in this thesis, a number of further investigations could
be conducted to further enlighten the possibilities of employing ECAs to optimise the design of
oﬀshore steel substructures. A few ideas from the authors of this paper are mentioned below.
The ﬁndings of this thesis wold allow for a more accurate determination of the maximum allowable
wall thickness for MP structures, as prescribed in EN 1993-1-10. This implies employing the frac-
ture mechanical model and procedure developed in this thesis, to evaluate the limiting thicknesses
based on a much more detailed fracture mechanical assessment, with a speciﬁc PoF.
Given the ﬁndings of possible over prediction of the geometry functions by using Chapuilot, Raju
& Newman or Mehmanparast solutions, the use of commercial FM software, could possibly yield
more accurate results for the crack propagation phase for an exact MP geometry. The improve-
ments of the accuracy in fracture mechanical parameters that were obtained in the supplementary
FEA analyses would be employed directly in the crack growth analysis, and could be extracted for
use in a failure assessment diagram.
Additionally, probabilistic fracture mechanics could be used as a substitute for deterministic meth-
ods. This could allow for a statistical determination of the possible initial crack sizes from fab-
rication. The probabilistic procedure could beneﬁcially in time consider the improved PODs of
PAUT, which would further increase the accuracy. This could also further enable the possibility to
obtain a probability of failure much closer to the S-N curves - making for a much better comparison
foundation.
Introducing more accurate crack growth laws in the analysis would possibly yield more favourable
crack growth curves, and as a result a better estimate of the fatigue life, compared to the S-N
curves. This would also better resemble the actual crack growth behaviour.
An investigation should be made into the increased risk of buckling in cross section with fatigue
cracks. Studies have proven that upwards of 8% reduction of buckling capacity due to fatigue
cracks can be expected [87]. While this thesis only considered the combination of fatigue crack
development and brittle fracture, there inevitably are more phenomena that are aﬀected by the
presence of cracks.
For monopiles, buckling serves as another great challenge besides fatigue.
Therefore the inﬂuence of cracks on buckling and the interaction between the two would be of
great interest, especially for XXL monopiles in the oﬀshore wind sector.

Standard, DNVGL-ST-0126 – Edition April 2016

DNV GL AS
       
Guidance note:
Because fatigue life is inversely proportional to fatigue damage, the design fatigue factor can be applied as a divisor on the calculated 
characteristic fatigue life to obtain the calculated design fatigue life.
---e-n-d---of---g-u-i-d-a-n-c-e---n-o-t-e---
 
4.11.5.2  The design fatigue factors in Table 4-20 apply to structures or structural components with low 
consequence of failure and for which it can be demonstrated that the structure satisfies the requirement for 
structural integrity in the ALS post-damaged condition with failure in the actual joint as the specified 
damage. The design fatigue factors in Table 4-20 depend on the location of the structural detail and of the 
accessibility for inspection and repair. The relation between the level of inspection and the requirement for 
DFF is detailed in Sec.9. All surfaces designed to be inspection-free, regardless of whether they are 
accessible for inspection and repair, shall be treated as inaccessible in design. The requirement for 
DFF = 3.0 reflects an assumption of no or irregular inspections, regardless of accessibility. DFF = 1.0 or 
DFF = 2.0 may be used in the atmospheric and submerged zones if an inspection plan is established with 
documented inspection method and inspection intervals that will result in the same safety level as 
DFF = 3.0 without inspections.
4.11.5.3  System effects are present wherever many weld segments in a long weld are subject to the same 
loading condition, such that the potential fatigue failure in the weld will take place in the weakest segment 
along the weld, i.e. the segment with the lowest fatigue strength. For welds where system effects are 
present, the system effects shall be accounted for in design, e.g. by appropriately increasing the DFF relative 
to the values specified in Table 4-20. For further details, reference is made to DNVGL-RP-C203 App.D.
4.11.6   Design cumulative fatigue by material factor approach
4.11.6.1  Present section covers an alternative to the DFF approach described in [4.11.4] and [4.11.5].
4.11.6.2  Design cumulative fatigue damage DD can be calculated by Miner’s sum as

Appendices
A.7
FAD options 2 and 3 of BS7910
In the following, the two more complicated options opposing the simple option 1 FAD will be
presented. Common to both options is that detailed stress-strain data are required for application
of the options. This rules out the applicability of the options with regards to the scope of the
study within this thesis. The two options are still presented, since they enable for possible further
optimisation on the fracture mechanical procedure, given that true stress-strain data is available.
A.7.1
Option 2
Where mean uni-axial tensile true stress-strain data is available, at the speciﬁc assessment temper-
ature, an option 2 FAL can be established. The option 2 failure assessment curve is suitable for all
metals regardless of the stress-strain behaviour. Where there is a discontinuity in the stress-strain
curve, at the lower yield strength, there correspondingly is a discontinuity in the option 2 FAL,
which often is at Lr = 1.
In order to derive the Option 2 curve, detailed stress-strain data are needed, and especially at
strains below 1%. As a minimum the FAL should be calculated at values Lr =0.7, 0.9, 0.98, 1.0,
1.02 and 1.1. Also at a suﬃcient number of points to deﬁne the curve up to Lr,max. The FAL of
option 2 is deﬁned by:
f(Lr) =
Eεref
Lrσy
+ L3
rσy
2Eεref
−1/2
for Lr < Lr,max
(A.4)
f(Lr) = 0
for Lr ≥Lr,max
(A.5)
Where εref is the true strain at the true stress σref = Lrσy.
A.7.2
Option 3
The third failure assessment curve option, is speciﬁc to a particular material, geometry and loading.
This is achieved by using both elastic and elastic-plastic analyses on the ﬂawed structure as function
of the loads giving rise to primary stresses. That is the load that contribute to the evaluation of
Lr. Like the option 2 FAL, this option requires stress-strain data, as well as geometry input. The
option 3 FAL is given by:
f(Lr) =
r
Je
J
for Lr < Lr,max
(A.6)
f(Lr) = 0
for Lr ≥Lr,max
(A.7)
This curve is not suitable for general use, and is only recommended for speciﬁc cases as an
alternative approach to options 1 and 2.

Appendices

==PAGE:175==
Appendices
C
Developed Engineering Critical Assessment Tool
The Engineering Critical Assessment tool which has been developed will be described in the fol-
lowing. The tool consists of a set of Matlab scripts emplying an algorightm resembling a explicit
integration scheme. A ﬂow chart describing the ECA process of the tool in detail can be seen in
Figure C.1.
Figure C.1: Flowchart over ECA procedure of developed script
C.1
User Input
Prior to an analysis, the user needs to supply the tool with input. The geometry of the problem
must be deﬁned along with the initial ﬂaw dimensions. At the current state of the tool, SIF so-
lutions for quarter- and semi-elliptical surface ﬂaws in plates and semi-elliptical surface ﬂaws in
cylinders can be analysed using the tool. Dependent on the SIF solution, a plate thickness, and
width/radius and initial ﬂaw size is deﬁned.
The loading conditions should also be deﬁned. These consist of the primary stresses, both
bending and membrane (or weight function for SIFs where applicable). The secondary stresses
which can be of the same form as the primary stresses. Lastly the cyclic stresses should be deﬁned.
These can be inputted as Markov matrices containing variable amplitude loads, or just a simple
constant amplitude load.
The properties of the material include the yield and ultimate tensile strength of the steel, the
young’s modulus and the Poisson’s ratio. Additionally, fracture toughness can be inputted, or
143
==PAGE:176==
Appendices
estimated by Charpy-V results.
Alongside the choice of the SIF solution, speciﬁcation of the relevant Paris-law parameters
should be made.
Lastly, the increment tolerances should be deﬁned. These consist of the increment size N,
and the tolerance ε. Specifying low values for these two parameters will result in a more precise
analysis, but at the cost of computational time.
C.2
Analysis Procedure
The analysis procedure is depicted in Figure C.1. The ﬂaw is assessed in the failure assessment
diagram including both primary and secondary stresses. If the ﬂaw is within the boundaries of the
FAD, a crack propagation step is initiated, where an increase in both the crack depth and width
is estimated. The crack dimensions are updated, and the new ﬂaw dimensions are now assessed
in the FAD. If the ﬂaw still is within the boundaries of the FAD, the same procedure is repeated,
until the assessment point falls outside the FAD. Once this happens, the analysis is halted, and
the fatigue life time and subsequently the point at which risk of brittle fracture prevails has been
determined.
C.3
Built-in Program Functions
Move information regarding ECAT to appendix for in-depth view of functions ECAT utilises a
standard Paris crack growth law and allows to the user to specify a number of input parameters,
such as:
• A DEL, Markov matrix or load history
• An extreme load
• Welding residual stresses
• Material properties; Young’s Modulus, Charpy-V energies
• Member type and geometry
• Flaw type and geometry
• Crack growth parameters for various environmental conditions
• FAD option
144
==PAGE:177==

SINTAP: Determination of fracture toughness from
Charpy impact energy
This appendix will brieﬂy cover the interpolation of values to obtain T27J for the case treated in
this thesis. The values of 50 J and 34 J from longitudinal and transverse respectively at -60◦are
from Section 4.2.2.
Table G.1: Allowable interpolation values and limits [63]
T −T27J[◦]

Figure G.1: Allowable interpolation values and limits
Longitudinal
The energy of 50 J is used to interpolate between the assumed energies of 41 J with T −T27J = 10◦
and 61 J with T −T27J = 20◦from Table G.1. The interpolation yields T −T27J = 14.5◦, which
can be used to determine the equivalent T27J.
−60◦−T27J = 14.5◦
→
T27J = −74.5◦

==PAGE:188==
Appendices
Transverse
The energy of 34 J is used to interpolate between the assumed energies of 27 J with T −T27J = 0◦
and 41 J with T −T27J = 10◦from Table G.1. The interpolation yields T −T27J = 5◦, which can
be used to determine the equivalent T27J.
−60◦−T27J = 5◦
→
T27J = −65◦

Stress Magniﬁcation Factor Solution
H.1
DNVGL-RP-C210
Figure H.1 shows the relevant parameters for determining Mk for a double sided, FPBW. Mk is
determined for two locations, shown with dotted rec circles in Figure H.1, namely Mkma (Ex-
pression H.1) for the deepest point and Mkmc (Expression H.5) for the crack ends at the surface.
Table H.1 gives an overview of the parameters and their limitations. This section only covers the
solutions for as-welded joints, where 0.0 ≤
ρ
T < 0.1. The solutions presented in this section are
from DNVGL-RP-C210 [56].

Appendices
J
Defect Delivery Requirements for Steel Plates
Structural steels must comply with a set of delivery requirements regarding the surface condition of
the material. This is required to make sure that the material properties used in the structural ver-
iﬁcation procedures of the accompanying norms are valid. The general requirements for structural
steels are found in DNVGL-OS-B101 [88]. Cracks, injurious surface ﬂaws, shells, sand patches,
scabs, blisters, laminations and sharp edged seams that are visually evident on surface and/or
edge of the plate are not accepted and require repair. Acceptance criteria for other imperfections,
such as pitting, rolled-in scale, indentations roll marks, scratches and grooves, which may occur
under normal manufacturing conditions, shall comply with requirements found in EN 10163-2 [89].
The requirements presented here apply to all plates of thickness in the range: 3mm ≤t ≤400mm.
Within EN 10163-2, a classiﬁcation system is introduced where the delivery requirements and
repair conditions are divided into two classes. The class is deﬁned as either class A or class B,
class A being the strictest of the two. The maximum permissible imperfection depths for both
classes can be seen in Table J.1. According to [88] plates and wide ﬂats shall be considered under
requirements for class A.
Table J.1: Max permissible depth of discontinuities acc. to [89]
Nominal thickness
Max permissible imperfection depth

J.1
Class A
Imperfections
Discontinuities other than cracks, shell and seams that do not exceed the limits of Requirement 1
in Table J.1 are permissible irrespective of their number.
A surface area with discontinuities within the limits of Requirement 1 in Table J.1, but with
a remaining thickness beneath the discontinuities less than the minimum thickness as speciﬁed in
EN 10029 [90] and EN 10051 [91] is permissible with a maximum of 15% of the inspected surface.
Discontinuities other than cracks, shell and seams with a depth exceeding the limits of Table
J.1 for Requirement 1, but not exceeding the limits for Requirement 2 and of which the sum of
the aﬀected areas does not exceed 5% of the inspected surface, may be left unrepaired.
165
==PAGE:198==
Appendices
In this case a surface area with a remaining thickness beneath the discontinuities less than the
minimum thickness speciﬁed in [90] and [91] is permissible with a maximum of 2% of the inspected
surface.
Defects
Discontinuities with a depth exceeding the limits of Requirement 1, but not exceeding Requirement
2 in Table J.1, with an aﬀected surface area of more than 5% of the inspected surface shall be
repaired.
Discontinuities with a depth exceeding the limits of Requirement 2 in Table J.1 shall be repaired.
Discontinuities such as cracks, shell and seams which in general are deep and sharp, shall always
be repaired irrespective of their depth and number.
J.2
Class B
The requirements of Class A apply, with the expectation that the remaining thickness beneath the
discontinuities and repair ground areas shall not be less than the minimum permissible thicknesses
as speciﬁed in [90] and [91].
J.2.1
Additional thickness requirements
The thickness tolerance requirements of EN 10029 [90] can be seen summarised in Table J.2. As
seen in the table a separate thickness classiﬁcation system is introduced, ranging from class A to
D, where:
• Class A: minus thickness tolerances depending on the nominal thickness.
• Class B: Fixed minus tolerance of 0.3 mm.
• Class C: Fixed minus tolerance of 0.0 mm.
• Class D: Symmetrical tolerances.
It is up to the purchaser to specify which thickness tolerance class is required. If no class is stated,
class A applies.

Furthermore, EN 10051 [91] speciﬁes requirements only for plates of thicknesses up to 25mm -
Therefore no further information from this norm will be presented.
Additionally EN 10225-1 [92] presents quality requirements for weldable structural steels for
oﬀshore ﬁxed structures. Herein it is stated that all surfaces of steel plates shall be 100% visually
inspected, and that the surface condition must comply with EN 10163-2 [89]; Class A (sub-class
3) unless otherwise is agreed at the time of the order. Sub-class 3 implies that repair by welding
is not allowed.

Appendices
K
Brittle Fracture Assessment
The brittle fracture checks introduced in Section 4.3.2 are presented here in their entirety.
K.1
EN 1993-1-10
K.1.1
Relative Stress Magnitude
With the brittle fracture assessment being classiﬁed as an accidental limit state, extreme stresses,
with no partial safety factors should be applied. In the input report, a maximum ULS load has been
supplied. Applying this load, reduced to no safety factors, results in a stress of σALS = 105.6MPa.
The eﬀective yield stress for a 78mm specimen of S355 NL/ML steel, is fy(t) = 325 MPa.
Thus, the percentage of the yield stress is 105.6/325 = 0.325. The relative stress magnitude then
is σEd = 0.325fy(t).
A summary of all the contributions which amounts to the reference temperature TEd will be
made in the following.
The service temperature is as supplied in the Input report [16] Tmd = −10◦C.
The radiation loss contribution is a ﬁxed value prescribed by EN 1993-1-10: ∆TR = −5◦C.
The contribution ∆Tσ is for the geometry in question set to 0◦C.
The strain rate contribution ∆T ˙ε is set to 0◦C, since there is no rapid loading of the structure.
The pile driving case should also be veriﬁed against brittle fracture, where a strain rate contribu-
tion due to the impact loads would be expectd. Though no strain rate information was supplied
in the input report [16].
The cold forming contribution ∆Tεcf is determined by the following:
∆Tεcf = −3 · εcf
(K.1)
Where the degree of cold forming for cylinders can be determined by:
εcf =
t
2R · 100
(K.2)
Thus, the cold forming temperature contribution to the reference temperature becomes:
∆Tεcf = −3 ·
78
2 · 3500 · 100 = −3.34◦C
(K.3)
The ﬁnal reference temperature is ﬁnally determined:
TEd = −10◦C −5◦C + 0◦C + 0◦C −3.34◦C = −18.34◦C
(K.4)

K.1.2
Determination of Limiting Wall Thickness
Based on the reference stress and temperatures, the limiting wall thickness is to be determined
by table look-up in Table 2.1 of EN 1993-1-10. A snippet of the table, for the relevant steel type
can be seen in Figure K.1. Since neither the reference stress or temperature matches the exact
intervals of the table, interpolation may be performed. Firstly, interpolation between -10◦and -20◦
is performed for both a stress magnitude of 0.25fy(t) and 0.5fy(t).
Figure K.1: Limiting WT’s for interpolation
For 0.25fy(t), the limiting thickness at −18.34◦C is t = 138.32mm. For 0.5fy(t), the limiting
thickness at −18.34◦C is t = 200mm.
Finally, interpolation between the two stress magnitudes yield that the ﬁnal limiting wall thick-
ness for a relative stress magnitude of 0.325fy(t) is t = 181.5mm. Since the actual wall thickness is
78mm, this is below the limiting thickness, and the member is veriﬁed to have suﬃcient capacity
against brittle fracture.

DNVGL-ST-0126
